Mashhurat, Musallamat, and Maqbulat
Definition of three non-certain premise types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three types of non-certain premises (mashhurat, musallamat, maqbulat) used in Islamic dialectic and rhetoric.

Core Idea
Not all premises are certain; these three are accepted by consensus, concession, or authority.

In Islamic logic, premises for arguments are not always demonstratively certain. For dialectical debate and rhetorical persuasion, logicians accept three types of premises that are not yaqini but are still useful. This passage defines mashhurat (well-known), musallamat (conceded), and maqbulat (accepted from authority). Understanding these categories helps you recognise what kind of argument you are dealing with and how to evaluate its strength.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common genus
All three are types of qadaya (propositions) that are not certain but are used in argument. Note that they are distinct from yaqiniyyat (certain premises).
2
Distinguish mashhurat
Look for propositions that are accepted either by all people (like ethical principles) or by a specific group (like cultural norms). Example: 'Beneficence is good' is accepted universally.
Mashhurat are based on widespread agreement, not truth.
3
Distinguish musallamat
Look for propositions that are conceded by an opponent in a debate for the sake of argument, or proven in one science and assumed in another. Example: 'The world is temporally originated' might be conceded by a debater.
Musallamat are context-dependent and may not be true in themselves.
4
Distinguish maqbulat
Look for propositions accepted from a trusted authority, such as a saint or sage. Example: A statement from a respected scholar about the benefits of a certain practice.
Maqbulat rely on the credibility of the source.
5
Practice classifying examples
Given a statement, decide whether it is mashhurat, musallamat, or maqbulat based on the grounds of acceptance.
Use the examples in the text: 'Beneficence is good' is mashhurat, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله من المشهورات هي القضايا التي تطابق فيها آراء الكل كحسن الاحسان و قبح العدوان او آراء طائفة كقبح ذبح الحيوانات عند اهل الهند. قوله « والمسلمات»: هي القضايا التي سلمت من الخصم في المناظرة او برهن عليها في علم و اخذت في آخر على سبيل التسليم. قوله من المقبولات» هي القضايا التي تؤخذ عمن يعتقد فيه كالاولياء والحكماء.
His saying 'from the well-known (mashhurat)': They are propositions in which the opinions of all coincide, like the goodness of beneficence and the badness of aggression, or the opinions of a group, like the badness of slaughtering animals among the people of India. His saying 'and the musallamat': They are propositions that have been conceded by the opponent in a disputation, or have been demonstrated in one science and taken over in another as accepted. His saying 'from the maqbulat': They are propositions that are accepted from one in whom one trusts, like saints and sages.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage defines three categories of premises that are not certain (yaqini) but are used in dialectical and rhetorical syllogisms. 'Mashhurat' (well-known) are propositions accepted by everyone or by a particular group, e.g., universal ethical principles like 'beneficence is good' or cultural norms like 'killing animals is bad' among Indians. 'Musallamat' (conceded) are propositions granted by an opponent in a debate (munazara) for the sake of argument, or propositions proven in one discipline and assumed in another. 'Maqbulat' (accepted) are propositions received from trusted authorities like saints (awliya) and sages (hukama). These categories are essential for building arguments that are persuasive even if not demonstratively certain. The examples are drawn from ethics, cultural practices, and scholarly authority, which are appropriate within the Islamic intellectual tradition.

Defining 'Consequent upon the Thing Essentially'
Author explains the definition of 'consequent upon the thing essentially'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a predicate is a 'lāḥiq bi dhāt' according to the criteria of 'without intermediary' and including coextensive accidents.

Core Idea
Essential accidents include both primary and coextensive accidents; both are 'consequent upon the thing essentially'.

In this passage, the commentator explains the phrase 'consequent upon them for their essences' and clarifies that it does not apply only to primary accidents but to all essential accidents, including those that occur through a coextensive term. This will help you understand the precise scope of scientific predicates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition being defined
The condition is 'لاحق للشيء اولاً وبالذات' (consequent upon the thing first and essentially). Find where it is defined in the passage.
It appears after the discussion of principles and premises.
2
Extract the definition
The definition given is 'بدون واسطة في العروض' (without an intermediary in occurrence). What does this mean? It means the predicate attaches directly to the subject without an intermediate attribute.
3
Check the exception
The passage notes that this definition does not include the accidental by means of a coextensive term, but then states that coextensive accidents are still considered essential accidents by agreement. How is this resolved? The commentator reinterprets the condition to include them.
The phrase 'أو لا يساويها' suggests a broader interpretation.
4
Understand the commentator's interpretation
The commentator explains that it refers to a specific preparedness, and that 'لاحق للشيء بما هو هو' includes all essential accidents. This broadens the scope beyond the strict 'without intermediary'.
This interpretation aligns with the author's commentary on al-Shamsiyya.
5
Apply to an example
Take the example from grammar: 'Every agent is nominative'. Is 'nominative' a lāḥiq bi dhāt for 'agent'? According to the broad interpretation, yes, because being nominative is an essential accident of agent (every agent of a complete verb is nominative).
This shows the practical application of the condition.

قوله ومحمولاتها: أي محمولات المسائل أمور خارجة عنها: أي عن موضوعات المسائل. لاحقة لها أي عارضة لتلك الموضوعات، والمراد ههنا محمولة عليها فان العارض هو الخارج المحمول فاذا جرد عن قيد الخروج للتصريح به قبل، بقى الحمل ولو اكتفى المصنف باللحوق لكفى و يوجد في بعض النسخ: قوله لذواتها: و هو بحسب الظاهر لا ينطبق إلا على العرض الأولى. وقد يقال المبادى لما يبدء به قبل المقصود والمقدمات لما يتوقف عليه الشروع على وجه الخبرة و فرط الرغبة كتعريف العلم وبيان غايته وموضوعه وكان اللاحق للشيء اولاً وبالذات اى بدون واسطة فى العروض ولا يشتمل على العارض بواسطة المساوى مع انه من العرض الذاتى اتفاقاً ولذا اوله بعض الشارحين و قال: اي لاستعداد مخصوص بذواتها سواء كان لحوقه اياها لذاتها اولا يساويها فان اللاحق للشيء بما هو هو يتناول الاعراض الذاتية جميعاً على ما قال المصنف فى شرح الرسالة الشمسية. ثم ان هذا القيد يدل على ان المصنف اختار مذهب الشيخ فى لزوم كون محمولات المسائل اعراضاً ذاتية لموضوعاتها و اليه ينظر كلام شارح المطالع لكن الاستاد المحقق قدس سره اورد عليه انه كثيراً ما يكون محمول المسألة بالنسبة الى موضوعها من الاعراض العامة الغريبة كقول الفقهاء: «كل مسكر حرام) وقول النحاة كل فاعل مرفوع و قول الطبيعيين: «كل فلك متحرك على الاستدارة». نعم يعتبر ان لايكون اعم من موضوع العلم. وصرح بذلك المحقق الطوسى ايضاً في «نقد التنزيل». واقول فى تزوم هذا الاعتبار ايضاً نظر لصحة ارجاع المحمولات العامة الى العرض الذاتي بالقيود المخصصة كما يرجع المحمولات الخاصة اليه بالمفهوم المردد و باعتبار الثاني، فعدم اعتبار الأول تحكم وهيهنا زيادة كلام.
His saying: 'and their predicates': i.e., the predicates of the problems are extraneous matters apart from them: i.e., from the subjects of the problems. 'Consequent upon them' i.e., accidental to those subjects, and what is intended here is predicated upon them, for the accidental is the extraneous predicated; so when the qualification of being extraneous is removed because it has been explicitly stated before, the predication remains. And if the author had sufficed with 'consequent' it would have been sufficient. And it is found in some copies: his saying 'for their essences' (li-dhatiha): and it apparently applies only to the primary accident. And it could be said: Principles are what one begins with before the intended goal, and premises are what the commencement depends upon, in the manner of expertise and extreme desire, such as defining the science, stating its purpose and subject. And 'consequent upon the thing first and essentially' i.e., without an intermediary in occurrence, and does not include the accidental by means of a coextensive [middle], even though it is an essential accident by agreement; therefore some commentators explained it saying: it refers to a specific preparedness in the things themselves, whether its consequence attaches to them for their essence or not equally, for that which is consequent upon a thing insofar as it is itself (bi ma huwa huwa) includes all essential accidents according to what the author stated in his commentary on al-Shamsiyya. Then this condition indicates that the author chose the doctrine of the Shaykh regarding the necessity of the predicates of the problems being essential accidents to their subjects, and towards this points the statement of the commentator of al-Mutali'. But the teacher, may his secret be sanctified, objected to him that often the predicate of a problem in relation to its subject is among the common extraneous accidents, such as the jurists' saying: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden', the grammarians' saying: 'Every agent is nominative', and the physicists' saying: 'Every celestial sphere is moving in a circle'. Yes, it is required that it not be more general than the subject of the science. And the Tusi, may God have mercy on him, also stated this in 'Naqd al-Tanzil'. And I say: regarding the necessity of this condition also there is consideration because the common predicates can be reduced to the essential accident through restricting qualifications, just as the particular predicates are referred to it through the ambiguous concept and by the second consideration; thus not considering the first is arbitrary. And there is further discussion here that cannot be accommodated.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage provides detailed commentary on the phrase 'lāḥiqatan lahā li-dhātihā' and discusses a significant objection. The author first explains that 'consequent upon them' means accidental to the subjects, and the intended meaning is 'predicated upon them'. The qualification 'extraneous' was added for clarity, but could be omitted. Then he notes a variant reading 'li-dhātihā' which apparently only applies to the primary accident. The author then defines 'consequent upon the thing first and essentially' as occurring without an intermediary in the act of being predicated, and excludes accidents that occur through a coextensive term, though they are still essential accidents. He references the author's commentary on al-Shamsiyya for this definition. The condition 'li-dhātihā' indicates the author follows Ibn Sina's position that predicates in sciences must be essential accidents. However, the teacher (al-muḥaqqiq) objects, citing examples from jurisprudence, grammar, and physics where the predicate is a common accident: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (forbidden is common to many), 'Every agent is nominative' (nominative case is a common accident of agent), 'Every celestial sphere is moving in circles' (circular motion is common). He responds that such predicates should not be more general than the subject of the science. Tusi also stated this. The author of the Hashiya adds that this condition is not necessary because common predicates can be reduced to essential accidents by adding specific qualifications, just as particular predicates are reduced via the ambiguous concept and the second consideration. So not requiring the condition is arbitrary.

Key Terms
لاحق
عرض ذاتي
اعراض عامة غريبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true about coextensive accidents (like 'forbidden' with 'intoxicant')?
A) They are excluded from being essential accidents.
B) They are not considered 'first and essentially' consequent.
C) They are included as essential accidents by agreement.
D) They are considered common accidents.

Key Takeaway
Essential accidents include both primary and coextensive accidents, and the term 'consequent upon the thing essentially' is interpreted to cover all essential accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the predicate 'forbidden' in 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' is a lāḥiq bi dhāt for 'intoxicant' according to the strict definition without intermediary. (Hint: 'forbidden' attaches to intoxicant through its intoxicating property, which is coextensive.) Then explain how the broad interpretation would classify it.

Critique of the Requirement for Essential Accident Predicates
Author critiques the requirement that all predicates be essential accidents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the objection that many scientific predicates are common accidents and explain how they can be reduced to essential accidents through qualifications.

Core Idea
Common predicates are used in sciences but can be reduced to essential accidents by adding specific qualifications, making the requirement flexible.

The commentator raises a powerful objection against Ibn Sina's position: many predicates in jurisprudence, grammar, and physics are common accidents, not specific to the subject. The response offers a way to reduce these to essential accidents, showing the requirement is not as strict as it seems.

Guided Path
1
State the position being critiqued
The position is that predicates of problems in a science must be essential accidents (a'radh dhatiyya) to the subject of that science. This is attributed to Ibn Sina (the Shaykh) and supported by the commentator of al-Mutali'.
2
Identify the objection's examples
Find the three examples from fiqh, nahw, and tabi'iyyat: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden', 'Every agent is nominative', 'Every celestial sphere is moving in a circle'.
These are from the teacher (al-muhaqqiq)'s objection.
3
Analyze why these are considered common accidents
'Forbidden' applies to many things beyond intoxicants (e.g., homicide, theft); 'nominative' is a grammatical case for many nouns (e.g., subject, predicate, etc.); 'circular motion' is not unique to spheres (e.g., wheels also rotate). So they are common extraneous accidents.
4
Examine the response
The response is that these predicates are not more general than the subject of the science (the science deals with a specific domain), and that common predicates can be reduced to essential accidents via restricting qualifications (qayud mukhassisa) or by considering them under the ambiguous concept.
The author of the Hashiya adds that this reduction is possible, and not requiring it is arbitrary.
5
Evaluate the reduction claim
How does the reduction work? For example, 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' can be seen as 'Every intoxicant is forbidden because it is intoxicating', where 'forbidden because intoxicating' becomes a property coextensive with intoxicant. Thus it is reduced to an essential accident.
The author argues that just as particular predicates are reduced via the ambiguous concept, common predicates are reduced via qualifications.

The Judgmental Proposition: Complete Declarative vs Restrictive Relation
Author argues that the judgmental proposition must be a complete declarative statement, not a restrictive relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the complete declarative proposition from the restrictive relation and identify why the judgmental proposition must be the former.

Core Idea
The judgmental proposition is a complete declarative, not a restrictive relation.

This passage addresses a key debate in logic: what kind of relation constitutes the object of judgment? Some argue that even negative judgments involve a restrictive affirmative relation, but the author argues forcefully that it must be a complete declarative proposition. We will examine the reasoning and the consensus of philosophers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rival conceptions
The judgmental proposition is defined either as a complete declarative (nisba tāmma khabariyya) or a restrictive affirmative relation (nisba taqyīdiyya thubūtiyya). The former is a full sentence capable of truth/falsehood; the latter is a qualifying relation within a concept.
2
Understand the objection to the restrictive view
Some argue that if the judgmental proposition in a negative sentence were restrictive, it would convert the negative into an affirmative when the subject exists, which is problematic.
3
Analyze the author's counter-argument
The author points out that the philosophers agreed that conceiving the judgmental proposition is a condition for judgment. This condition only holds if the proposition is a complete declarative, because one could conceive the complete declarative and then assent without considering any restrictive relation.
4
Test with an example: 'Zayd is not a writer'
In this negative sentence, if the judgmental proposition were a restrictive affirmative relation, you would first conceive the relation between Zayd and writer restrictively, then affirm or deny. But the author says you can conceive the complete declarative 'Zayd is not a writer' and then assent without noticing any restrictive relation. So the judgmental proposition must be the complete declarative.
5
Conclude with the author's verdict
The author states: 'The truth is that it is a complete declarative.' This is supported by the consensus of philosophers and the logical analysis of how assent occurs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۲۱) فسر النسبة الحكمية بالنسبة التامة الخبرية الثبوتية في الموجبة والسالبة في السالبة، ومنهم من ذهب الى انها يجب ان يكون ثبوتية تقييدية فيهما والا يعبر السالبة موجبة عند وجود الموضوع وفيه نظر لانه انما كانت الحكمية النسبة لو كانت النسبة الحكمية تقييدية كما اعترف به و ملحوظة تفصيلاً على وجه تكون محكوماً يصبح عليها كما اذا قلنا النسبة بين الطرفين باللا ثبوت ليست بواقعة و اما اذا كانت قامة خبرية وغير ملحوظة تفصيلاً كما يفهم من قولنا زيد ليس بكاتب و ادركتها ثم اذ عنتها وقبلتها فلا . والحق انها تامة خبرية، لان الحكماء اتفقوا على ان تصور النسبة الحكمية شرط الحصول الحكم وهذا اما الاتفاق منهم اذا كانت النسبة الحكمية هى النسبة التامة الخبرية. لأنه ما لم يحصل تلك النسبة يصح فى الذهن لم يكن له الاذعان الذى هو من ضروريات الحكم و اما اذا التقييدية الثبوتية فلا، اذ يمكن بعد تصور الطرفين تصور النسبة التامة الخبرية بينهما بلا اذعان ثم مع الاذعان من غير ملاحظة نسبة تقييدية بينهما اصلاً وذلك ظاهر لمن راجع وجدانه . (عبد الرحيم)
(21) He interpreted the judgmental proposition as the complete declarative affirmative proposition in the affirmative, and the negative in the negative. Some took the view that it must be a restrictive affirmative in both, otherwise the negative would become affirmative when the subject exists. But there is a consideration. For the judgmental proposition would only be if the judgmental proposition were restrictive, as he acknowledged, and it is noticed in detail such that it becomes a subject of judgment, as when we say: 'The relation between the two extremes is not real'. But if it is a complete declarative proposition and not noticed in detail, as understood from our saying: 'Zayd is not a writer' and you apprehend it then assent to it and accept it, then no. The truth is that it is a complete declarative, because the philosophers agreed that the conception of the judgmental proposition is a condition for obtaining the judgment. And this agreement of theirs is only if the judgmental proposition is the complete declarative proposition. Because unless that proposition is obtained in the mind, he would not have the assent which is among the necessities of judgment. But if it is the restrictive affirmative, then no, for it is possible after conceiving the two extremes to conceive the complete declarative proposition between them without assent, then with assent without noticing any restrictive relation between them at all. That is apparent to one who consults his conscience.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage discusses a dispute about the nature of the judgmental proposition (nisba ḥukmiyya) that forms the object of assent. Some argue it must be a restrictive affirmative relation (thubūtiyya taqyīdiyya) even in negative judgments, otherwise a negative would be converted to an affirmative when the subject exists. The author, however, argues that the correct view is that the judgmental proposition is a complete declarative proposition (nisba tāmma khabariyya). He appeals to the consensus of the philosophers that the conception of the judgmental proposition is a condition for judgment, and this condition can only be fulfilled by a complete declarative proposition. If it were a restrictive relation, one could conceive the complete proposition separately and then assent without attending to the restrictive relation, which contradicts the condition. The passage ends by asserting that this is evident to anyone who consults their conscience (wijdān).

Key Terms
نسبة حكمية
نسبة تامة خبرية
نسبة تقييدية ثبوتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the view that the judgmental proposition is a restrictive affirmative relation?
A) Because it would make negative judgments impossible.
B) Because the philosophers agree it is a complete declarative.
C) Because restrictive relations cannot be assented to.
D) Because it leads to circularity.

Key Takeaway
The judgmental proposition is a complete declarative because only such a proposition can serve as the object of assent, as agreed by the philosophers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
2. Explain why the judgmental proposition in the statement 'Allah is not unjust' must be a complete declarative, using the author's argument from the consensus of philosophers.

Three Parts or Four? The Ancient vs Later Philosophers on Proposition Composition
Presents the dispute between ancient and later philosophers on proposition parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two positions on the composition of the proposition and explain the logical reasoning behind each, using the case of doubt.

Core Idea
The proposition's composition hinges on whether the occurrence of the relation is a separate part or merely an aspect of perception.

The ancient philosophers held that a proposition consists of three parts: subject, predicate, and the relational copula. The later philosophers added a fourth: the occurrence or non-occurrence of that relation. Their argument hinges on the experience of doubt, where one perceives the relation without judgment. This lesson unpacks the debate and its resolution through the concept of acquiescence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three-part structure
According to the ancients, the proposition has three parts: subject (المحكوم عليه), predicate (المحكوم به), and the perfect predicative relation (النسبة التامة الخبرية). The perception of this relation constitutes the judgment (الحكم).
The relation is the copula linking subject and predicate.
2
Understand the later philosophers' argument
The later philosophers observe that in doubt, one perceives the relation but has no judgment. When one perceives the occurrence or non-occurrence of that relation, judgment occurs. Thus, occurrence is a distinct perception, making a fourth part.
Their reasoning uses the example of doubt (صورة الشك).
3
Analyze the objection
The objection states that the perception in doubt is the same perception as in judgment, just without acquiescence (إذعان). The difference is in the mode, not in additional parts.
This objection preserves the three-part structure while explaining the psychological difference.
4
Synthesize the reconciliation
The reconciliation proposes that the judgmental relation can be perceived without acquiescence (as conceptual knowledge) or with acquiescence (as assenting knowledge). Thus, the proposition has three parts in essence and four in detailed consideration.
This shows that the two views are compatible at different levels of analysis.

المقام الثانى فى تركيب القضية، واختلف فيه ايضاً , فالقدماء من الحكماء على أنها مركبة من اجزاء المحكوم عليه و به والنسبة التامة الخبرية وادراكها هو الحكم التي يعبر عنها بالوقوع واللا وقوع والمتأخرون على أنها مركبة من امور اربعة هي المحكوم عليه و به والنسبة التقييدية الثبوتية و وقوع تلك النسبة اولا وقوعها لما رأوا أنه يوجد في صورة الشك تصور النسبة ولا يوجد الحكم فاذا ادرك وقوع تلك النسبة او لا وقوعها فقد حصل الحكم وزان الشك قالوا: فهذا ادراك آخر مغاير للادراك ضرورة، وقد نوقش فيه بان الدرك في صورة الشك هو بعينه الدرك فى صورة الحكم غير انه ادرك فى الاولى بادراك غير اذعانى و في الثانية بإدراك اذعانى هذا لا يوجب زيادة الاجزاء فان التفاوت بين الادراكين باعتبار المتعلق. ويمكن التوفيق بما ذكره بعض المتأخرين بان النسبة الحكمية تارة يتعلق بها الادراك بدون الاذعان و هي بهذا الاعتبار من المعلومات التصورية ويسمى النسبة الحكمية وتارة مع الاذعان وهي بهذا الاعتبار من المعلومات التصديقية على مذهب الحكيم الحكيم ويسمى الحكم.
و اكتفاء المحشى (ره) في بيان تركيب التصديق بالطرفين والحكم، مبنى على هذا والاعتباران متغایران فمن قال بتركيبها من الأربعة لاحظ التعدد الاعتبارى ومن لم يقل بذلك لاحظ الاتحاد الذاتي و الى هذا اشار شارح المطالع حيث قال ان اجزاء القضية عند التفصيل اربعة كما ان حاصل كلام المحشى ان المصنف اختار في المقامين مذهب القدماء اما فى الاول فلجعله التصديق نفس الاذعان و الحكم و اما فى الثانى فلجعله متعلق الاذعان هو النسبة التامة الخبرية دون الوقوع واللا وقوع وانما وصف متعلق الحكم بكونه جزء اخيراً للقضية بينهما على استلزام مذهب القدماء تثليث اجزاء القضية، بيان ذلك : انه لما كانت هذه النسبة جزء اخيراً للقضية ولا شك في انه ليس المعتبر في حصول التصديق الا تصوران على تلك النسبة فتنحصر اجزائها في الثلاثة واما اذا لم يكن جزء اخيراً لها فلا يلزم تثليث اجزائها لجواز ان يكون لها جزء اخيراً مؤخراً عن تلك النسبة. ولما كان لقائل ان يقول ان في الكلام مضافاً محذوفاً و : التقدير ان كان اذعاناً لوقوع النسبة، فتكون اجزاء القضية عنده في التحقيق اربعة، دفع ذلك بان
في مقدمة علم المنطق المصنف سيشير فى مباحث القضايا على تثليث اجزاء القضية، و ما ذكره فى مباحث القضايا لاينا في ما ذكره هيهنا من بساطة التصديق لان القضية غير التصديق فافهم وهيهنا مباحث طويل الاذيال لا يليق تفصيلها بهذه الحاشية. (عبد الرحيم)
(۲۴) الاولى ان يزيد عليه تصور النسبة ,الحكمية، فان قول الامام انه المركب من الأمور الأربعة دون الثلاثة كما عرفت لكنه لما لم يكن مقصوده تحقیق مذهبه بل بيان الافتراق بينه وبين ما ذهب اليه الحكماء، لم يتوجه اليه فتأمل (محمد على)
(۲۵) قوله و اختار مذهب القدماء :اعلم ان الحكماء بعد ما اتفقوا على ان التصديق هو نفس الحكم والاذعان دون المركب منه و من التصورات الثلاث اختلفوا فى ان القضية هل هي مركبة من الامور الثلاثة اعنى تصور المحكوم عليه و به و النسبة الخبريه الثبوتية او السلبية أو من الأمور الأربعة الثلاثة المذكورة وتصور وقوع تلك النسبة اولا وقوعها ؟ وذهب المتقدمون الى الاول والمتأخرون الى الثانى. والمصنف اختار الاول حيث جعل متعلق الاذعان و التصديق نفس النسبة الحكمية لا وقوعها اولا وقوعها كما جعله اياه من ذهب الى الثانى (محمد على)
(۲۶) بفتح اللام اى ما يتعلق به الاذعان وقد يصحف بكسرها ولا يخفى بعده. (محمد على) (۲۷) صفة للمتعلق لاللاذعان ضرورة ان الاذعان والتصديق ليس جزء للقضية بل متعلق بالجزء الاخير منها . (محمد على)
(۲۸) قوله و الحكم الذى هو الجزء الأخير للقضية اى و جعل متعلق الحكم الذى هو الجزء الأخير للقضية هو النسبة الخبرية بمعنى ان الحكم بالنسبة الخيرية متعلق بالنسبة المذكورة بالضرورة. (التقريب ص۱۶)
(۲۹) قوله لا وقوع النسبة الثبوتية التقييدية: لا يخفى استدراك هذا القيد والاولى ان يذكر بدلها الخبرية كما هو ظاهر و كذا لا معنى للتقييد بقوله التقييدية، ضرورة ان النسبة الخبرية لا تكون الا تقييدية فافهم (ميرزا محمد على
وقال الاستاذ الشيخ محمد الكرمى دامت افاضاته فى هذا المورد تقييد النسب التامة بالتقييدية لغو لان النسب التامة واحدة للتقييد حتماً وهو تقييد موضوعها بمحمولها ونوعاً لا تطلق النسب التقييدية الا على النسب الناقصة كالنسب الاضافية والوصفية وما هو على وتيرتها التقريب ص۱۶) (۳۰) قوله وقوع النسبة اولا وقوعها ان كان المراد بالوقوع واللا وقوع هو الايجاب و السلب الظاهرين في القضية الملفوظة أو المتخطرين في القضية المضمرة في النفس فذلك عبارة أخرى عن قولهم النسبة الثبوتية و النسبة السلبية و ان كان المراد بالوقوع واللا وقوع هو الثبوت الواقعي و استقرار مادة القضية في الواقع وعدم استقرارها و ثبوتها كذلك , فهذا امر غير قضية السلب و الايجاب الظاهرين في القضية الملفوظة وغير المتخطرين فى القضية المضمرة , فان السلب و الايجاب الملفوظين والمتخطرين قد يخالفان الواقع بالبداهة. ومحط اذعان النفس بالضرورة هو النسبة التي انكشف عنها الواقع بايجاب او بسلب. و اما نفس النسبة السالبة او الموجبة غير ملحوظ بها انكشاف الواقع عنها فاذعان النفس بها قد يكون كاذعانها للتمويهات والاضاليل وعلى كل حال فوقوع النسبة ان كان باعتبار ايجابها اللفظي او
The second topic: on the composition of the proposition. There is also disagreement in it. The ancient philosophers hold that it is composed of the parts: the subject, the predicate, and the perfect predicative relation; and its perception is the judgment which is expressed by occurrence and non-occurrence. The later philosophers hold that it is composed of four matters: the subject, the predicate, the restrictive affirmative relation, and the occurrence or non-occurrence of that relation. Because they saw that in the case of doubt, the perception of the relation exists but the judgment does not. So when one perceives the occurrence or non-occurrence of that relation, the judgment is obtained, as in the case of doubt. They said: This is another perception different from the first perception necessarily. It has been objected that the perception in the case of doubt is the same perception as in the case of judgment, except that in the first it is perceived with non-acquiescent perception, and in the second with acquiescent perception. This does not necessitate an increase in parts, for the difference between the two perceptions is due to the object. Reconciliation is possible by what some later scholars mentioned: The judgmental relation is sometimes perceived without acquiescence, and in this respect it is among conceptual knowledge and is called judgmental relation; sometimes it is perceived with acquiescence, and in this respect it is among assenting knowledge according to the philosopher's doctrine and is called judgment. And the commentator's (may God have mercy on him) sufficing in explaining the composition of tasdiq as the two extremes and the judgment is based on this. The two considerations are different: whoever says it is composed of four observes the multiplicity in consideration, and whoever does not say that observes the unity in essence. The commentator on al-Matali' pointed to this where he said that the parts of the proposition in detail are four, just as the conclusion of the commentator's words is that the author chose in both topics the doctrine of the ancients: in the first by making tasdiq the same as acquiescence and the judgment; in the second by making the object of acquiescence the perfect predicative relation without occurrence or non-occurrence. He only described the object of the judgment as being the final part of the proposition between them on the entailment of the ancients' doctrine of three parts of the proposition. Explanation: Since this relation is the final part of the proposition, and there is no doubt that the only thing considered in obtaining tasdiq is two perceptions of that relation, so its parts are restricted to three. But if it is not a final part for it, then three parts are not necessary, because it is possible that it has a final part posterior to that relation. Since it is possible for someone to say that there is an omitted genitive in the statement, and the estimation is 'if there is acquiescence to the occurrence of the relation', then the parts of the proposition according to him in reality are four. He repelled this by saying: In the introduction to the science of logic, the author will indicate in the topics of propositions the three parts of the proposition. And what he mentioned in the topics of propositions does not conflict with what he mentioned here of the simplicity of tasdiq, because the proposition is not the tasdiq. So understand. And there are lengthy discussions here whose details are not suitable for this hashiya. (Abd al-Rahim)
(24) It is better to add to it the perception of the judgmental relation. For the Imam's statement that it is composed of four matters without the three, as you know, but since his intention was not to verify his own doctrine but to explain the difference between it and what the philosophers held, he did not attend to that. So reflect. (Muhammad Ali)
(25) His statement: 'and he chose the doctrine of the ancients': Know that the philosophers, after they agreed that tasdiq is the judgment and acquiescence itself, not the composite of it and the three tasawwurs, differed as to whether the proposition is composed of three matters—namely, the perception of the subject, the predicate, and the affirmative or negative predicative relation—or of the four matters: the three mentioned and the perception of the occurrence or non-occurrence of that relation. The ancients went for the first, the later for the second. And the author chose the first, making the object of acquiescence and tasdiq the judgmental relation itself, not its occurrence or non-occurrence, as did the one who went for the second. (Muhammad Ali)
(26) With fatha on the lam: i.e., that to which acquiescence is attached; it may be misread with kasra, and its distance is not hidden. (Muhammad Ali)
(27) An adjective for the object, not for acquiescence, necessarily because acquiescence and tasdiq are not part of the proposition but are attached to its final part. (Muhammad Ali)
(28) His statement: 'and the judgment which is the final part of the proposition': i.e., he made the object of the judgment, which is the final part of the proposition, the predicative relation, meaning that the judgment is necessarily attached to the mentioned relation. (al-Taqrib p.16)
(29) His statement: 'not the occurrence of the restrictive affirmative relation': It is not hidden that this qualification is unnecessary; it is better to use 'predicative' instead, as is apparent. Similarly, there is no meaning to restricting it to 'restrictive', because the predicative relation can only be restrictive. So understand. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
And the teacher Sheikh Muhammad al-Karmi (may his blessings continue) said in this regard: Restricting perfect relations to 'restrictive' is pointless, because perfect relations are necessarily restrictive, being restrictions of the subject by the predicate, and the term 'restrictive relations' is only used for incomplete relations, like additive and descriptive relations and what follows their pattern. (al-Taqrib p.16)
(30) His statement: 'the occurrence or non-occurrence of the relation': If what is meant by occurrence and non-occurrence is the affirmation and negation apparent in the spoken proposition or the implicit proposition in the mind, then that is just another expression for 'affirmative relation' and 'negative relation'. If what is meant is real existence and the stability of the matter of the proposition in reality and its non-existence, then this is outside the question of the negation and affirmation apparent in the spoken proposition and the implicit proposition. For the spoken and implicit negation and affirmation may contradict reality by necessity. The object of the soul's acquiescence is necessarily the relation which reality unveils by affirmation or negation. But the negative or affirmative relation itself, without regard to the unveiling of reality, the soul's acquiescence to it may be like its acquiescence to deceptions and falsehoods. In any case, occurrence of the relation if considered from the aspect of its verbal affirmation or...

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the second topic: the composition of the proposition (qadiyya). It presents the dispute between the ancient philosophers (qudama') and the later philosophers (muta'akhkhirun). The ancients held that the proposition has three parts: subject (mahkum 'alayh), predicate (mahkum bih), and the perfect predicative relation (al-nisba al-tamma al-khabariyya). The perception of this relation is the judgment (hukm), expressed as occurrence or non-occurrence. The later philosophers added a fourth part: the occurrence or non-occurrence of that relation. Their argument is that in cases of doubt, one perceives the relation but has no judgment; when one perceives the occurrence/non-occurrence, judgment occurs. So these are two distinct perceptions. This is critiqued: the perception in doubt is the same perception as in judgment, just without acquiescence (idh'ān). The difference lies in the manner of perception, not in additional parts. A reconciliation is suggested: the judgmental relation can be perceived without acquiescence (making it conceptual knowledge) or with acquiescence (making it assenting knowledge). The commentator (al-Muhashshi) builds on this, noting that the two considerations differ. The commentator on al-Matali' is quoted as saying that in detail the proposition has four parts, but the author (matn) chose the ancient view in both topics: tasdiq is acquiescence itself, and the object of acquiescence is the perfect predicative relation, not its occurrence. The passage then elaborates on the necessity of three parts if the relation is the final part, and defends against an objection about an omitted genitive. The marginal notes (numbered 24-30) add further clarification: 24 discusses the Imam's intention; 25 restates the two positions; 26-27 note linguistic points; 28-30 critique the phrase 'occurrence of the restrictive affirmative relation' and distinguish between verbal occurrence and real occurrence. The text ends with a discussion on the object of acquiescence being the relation unveiled by reality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later philosophers, what is the fourth part of the proposition?
A) The subject
B) The predicate
C) The perfect predicative relation
D) The occurrence or non-occurrence of the relation

Key Takeaway
The debate between three and four parts is resolved by recognizing that the proposition can be considered in two ways: essentially three parts, with occurrence as a conceptual distinction, or four parts in detailed analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the proposition 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (كل مسكر حرام), list the three parts according to the ancients and the four parts according to the later philosophers.
?
Explain the later philosophers' argument using the case of doubt: if someone doubts whether a particular drink is intoxicating, they perceive the relation 'intoxicant is forbidden' but do not judge; when they learn it is intoxicating, they perceive the occurrence of that relation, and judgment occurs.

Propositions
advanced
7 min
The Reconciliation: Acquiescence and the Unity of the Proposition
Provides the reconciliation and explains the two considerations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the debate on proposition parts is reconciled through the concept of acquiescence and identify the two considerations (essence vs. detail).

Core Idea
The proposition has three parts in essence and four in detail, depending on whether the relation is perceived with or without acquiescence.

The apparent conflict between the three-part and four-part views is resolved by the insight that the judgmental relation can be perceived in two modes: without acquiescence (conceptual) or with acquiescence (assent). The commentator refines this into a unified theory, showing that the two positions are complementary perspectives.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the role of acquiescence (إذعان)
Acquiescence is the act of mentally assenting to the relation. Without it, the relation is merely conceptual; with it, it becomes a judgment. The same relation can be perceived in both ways.
This is the key to reconciling the two views.
2
Understand the two considerations
When the relation is perceived without acquiescence, it is part of conceptual knowledge (تصورات). When perceived with acquiescence, it is part of assenting knowledge (تصديقات). The proposition as a linguistic entity can be analyzed in both ways.
The ancients focused on the essence (the relation itself), the later on the detailed parts (relation plus occurrence).
3
Apply the reconciliation to the proposition
The proposition can be said to have three parts in essence (subject, predicate, relation) and four parts in detail (subject, predicate, relation, occurrence). This is not a contradiction but a difference of analytic level.
The commentator on al-Matali' is quoted as saying that in detail the parts are four.

(۲۸) قوله و الحكم الذى هو الجزء الأخير للقضية اى و جعل متعلق الحكم الذى هو الجزء الأخير للقضية هو النسبة الخبرية بمعنى ان الحكم بالنسبة الخيرية متعلق بالنسبة المذكورة بالضرورة. (التقريب ص۱۶)
(۲۹) قوله لا وقوع النسبة الثبوتية التقييدية: لا يخفى استدراك هذا القيد والاولى ان يذكر بدلها الخبرية كما هو ظاهر و كذا لا معنى للتقييد بقوله التقييدية، ضرورة ان النسبة الخبرية لا تكون الا تقييدية فافهم (ميرزا محمد على
وقال الاستاذ الشيخ محمد الكرمى دامت افاضاته فى هذا المورد تقييد النسب التامة بالتقييدية لغو لان النسب التامة واحدة للتقييد حتماً وهو تقييد موضوعها بمحمولها ونوعاً لا تطلق النسب التقييدية الا على النسب الناقصة كالنسب الاضافية والوصفية وما هو على وتيرتها التقريب ص۱۶) (۳۰) قوله وقوع النسبة اولا وقوعها ان كان المراد بالوقوع واللا وقوع هو الايجاب و السلب الظاهرين في القضية الملفوظة أو المتخطرين في القضية المضمرة في النفس فذلك عبارة أخرى عن قولهم النسبة الثبوتية و النسبة السلبية و ان كان المراد بالوقوع واللا وقوع هو الثبوت الواقعي و استقرار مادة القضية في الواقع وعدم استقرارها و ثبوتها كذلك , فهذا امر غير قضية السلب و الايجاب الظاهرين في القضية الملفوظة وغير المتخطرين فى القضية المضمرة , فان السلب و الايجاب الملفوظين والمتخطرين قد يخالفان الواقع بالبداهة. ومحط اذعان النفس بالضرورة هو النسبة التي انكشف عنها الواقع بايجاب او بسلب. و اما نفس النسبة السالبة او الموجبة غير ملحوظ بها انكشاف الواقع عنها فاذعان النفس بها قد يكون كاذعانها للتمويهات والاضاليل وعلى كل حال فوقوع النسبة ان كان باعتبار ايجابها اللفظي او
The second topic: on the composition of the proposition. There is also disagreement in it. The ancient philosophers hold that it is composed of the parts: the subject, the predicate, and the perfect predicative relation; and its perception is the judgment which is expressed by occurrence and non-occurrence. The later philosophers hold that it is composed of four matters: the subject, the predicate, the restrictive affirmative relation, and the occurrence or non-occurrence of that relation. Because they saw that in the case of doubt, the perception of the relation exists but the judgment does not. So when one perceives the occurrence or non-occurrence of that relation, the judgment is obtained, as in the case of doubt. They said: This is another perception different from the first perception necessarily. It has been objected that the perception in the case of doubt is the same perception as in the case of judgment, except that in the first it is perceived with non-acquiescent perception, and in the second with acquiescent perception. This does not necessitate an increase in parts, for the difference between the two perceptions is due to the object. Reconciliation is possible by what some later scholars mentioned: The judgmental relation is sometimes perceived without acquiescence, and in this respect it is among conceptual knowledge and is called judgmental relation; sometimes it is perceived with acquiescence, and in this respect it is among assenting knowledge according to the philosopher's doctrine and is called judgment. And the commentator's (may God have mercy on him) sufficing in explaining the composition of tasdiq as the two extremes and the judgment is based on this. The two considerations are different: whoever says it is composed of four observes the multiplicity in consideration, and whoever does not say that observes the unity in essence. The commentator on al-Matali' pointed to this where he said that the parts of the proposition in detail are four, just as the conclusion of the commentator's words is that the author chose in both topics the doctrine of the ancients: in the first by making tasdiq the same as acquiescence and the judgment; in the second by making the object of acquiescence the perfect predicative relation without occurrence or non-occurrence. He only described the object of the judgment as being the final part of the proposition between them on the entailment of the ancients' doctrine of three parts of the proposition. Explanation: Since this relation is the final part of the proposition, and there is no doubt that the only thing considered in obtaining tasdiq is two perceptions of that relation, so its parts are restricted to three. But if it is not a final part for it, then three parts are not necessary, because it is possible that it has a final part posterior to that relation. Since it is possible for someone to say that there is an omitted genitive in the statement, and the estimation is 'if there is acquiescence to the occurrence of the relation', then the parts of the proposition according to him in reality are four. He repelled this by saying: In the introduction to the science of logic, the author will indicate in the topics of propositions the three parts of the proposition. And what he mentioned in the topics of propositions does not conflict with what he mentioned here of the simplicity of tasdiq, because the proposition is not the tasdiq. So understand. And there are lengthy discussions here whose details are not suitable for this hashiya. (Abd al-Rahim)
(24) It is better to add to it the perception of the judgmental relation. For the Imam's statement that it is composed of four matters without the three, as you know, but since his intention was not to verify his own doctrine but to explain the difference between it and what the philosophers held, he did not attend to that. So reflect. (Muhammad Ali)
(25) His statement: 'and he chose the doctrine of the ancients': Know that the philosophers, after they agreed that tasdiq is the judgment and acquiescence itself, not the composite of it and the three tasawwurs, differed as to whether the proposition is composed of three matters—namely, the perception of the subject, the predicate, and the affirmative or negative predicative relation—or of the four matters: the three mentioned and the perception of the occurrence or non-occurrence of that relation. The ancients went for the first, the later for the second. And the author chose the first, making the object of acquiescence and tasdiq the judgmental relation itself, not its occurrence or non-occurrence, as did the one who went for the second. (Muhammad Ali)
(26) With fatha on the lam: i.e., that to which acquiescence is attached; it may be misread with kasra, and its distance is not hidden. (Muhammad Ali)
(27) An adjective for the object, not for acquiescence, necessarily because acquiescence and tasdiq are not part of the proposition but are attached to its final part. (Muhammad Ali)
(28) His statement: 'and the judgment which is the final part of the proposition': i.e., he made the object of the judgment, which is the final part of the proposition, the predicative relation, meaning that the judgment is necessarily attached to the mentioned relation. (al-Taqrib p.16)
(29) His statement: 'not the occurrence of the restrictive affirmative relation': It is not hidden that this qualification is unnecessary; it is better to use 'predicative' instead, as is apparent. Similarly, there is no meaning to restricting it to 'restrictive', because the predicative relation can only be restrictive. So understand. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
And the teacher Sheikh Muhammad al-Karmi (may his blessings continue) said in this regard: Restricting perfect relations to 'restrictive' is pointless, because perfect relations are necessarily restrictive, being restrictions of the subject by the predicate, and the term 'restrictive relations' is only used for incomplete relations, like additive and descriptive relations and what follows their pattern. (al-Taqrib p.16)
(30) His statement: 'the occurrence or non-occurrence of the relation': If what is meant by occurrence and non-occurrence is the affirmation and negation apparent in the spoken proposition or the implicit proposition in the mind, then that is just another expression for 'affirmative relation' and 'negative relation'. If what is meant is real existence and the stability of the matter of the proposition in reality and its non-existence, then this is outside the question of the negation and affirmation apparent in the spoken proposition and the implicit proposition. For the spoken and implicit negation and affirmation may contradict reality by necessity. The object of the soul's acquiescence is necessarily the relation which reality unveils by affirmation or negation. But the negative or affirmative relation itself, without regard to the unveiling of reality, the soul's acquiescence to it may be like its acquiescence to deceptions and falsehoods. In any case, occurrence of the relation if considered from the aspect of its verbal affirmation or...
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the second topic: the composition of the proposition (qadiyya). It presents the dispute between the ancient philosophers (qudama') and the later philosophers (muta'akhkhirun). The ancients held that the proposition has three parts: subject (mahkum 'alayh), predicate (mahkum bih), and the perfect predicative relation (al-nisba al-tamma al-khabariyya). The perception of this relation is the judgment (hukm), expressed as occurrence or non-occurrence. The later philosophers added a fourth part: the occurrence or non-occurrence of that relation. Their argument is that in cases of doubt, one perceives the relation but has no judgment; when one perceives the occurrence/non-occurrence, judgment occurs. So these are two distinct perceptions. This is critiqued: the perception in doubt is the same perception as in judgment, just without acquiescence (idh'ān). The difference lies in the manner of perception, not in additional parts. A reconciliation is suggested: the judgmental relation can be perceived without acquiescence (making it conceptual knowledge) or with acquiescence (making it assenting knowledge). The commentator (al-Muhashshi) builds on this, noting that the two considerations differ. The commentator on al-Matali' is quoted as saying that in detail the proposition has four parts, but the author (matn) chose the ancient view in both topics: tasdiq is acquiescence itself, and the object of acquiescence is the perfect predicative relation, not its occurrence. The passage then elaborates on the necessity of three parts if the relation is the final part, and defends against an objection about an omitted genitive. The marginal notes (numbered 24-30) add further clarification: 24 discusses the Imam's intention; 25 restates the two positions; 26-27 note linguistic points; 28-30 critique the phrase 'occurrence of the restrictive affirmative relation' and distinguish between verbal occurrence and real occurrence. The text ends with a discussion on the object of acquiescence being the relation unveiled by reality.

Key Terms
اذعان
نسبة حكمية
اعتبار
اتحاد ذاتي

Checkpoint
According to the reconciliation, how can the proposition have both three and four parts?
A) The proposition literally has four parts, but three are essential.
B) It depends on whether the proposition is affirmative or negative.
C) Three parts in essence, four parts in detailed consideration.
D) The later philosophers were mistaken; there are only three parts.

Key Takeaway
The proposition's composition is a matter of perspective: essence vs. detail, reconciled by the presence or absence of acquiescence in perceiving the relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Parts of a Proposition: Tripartite vs. Quadripartite
Commentator refutes a misunderstanding about the parts of a proposition and the author's choice of the ancient view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three parts of a proposition according to the ancient doctrine and explain why occurrence/non-occurrence is not a separate part.

Core Idea
A proposition has three parts, not four: subject, predicate, and the relation.

This lesson examines a subtle point in the structure of propositions: whether the proposition consists of three parts (subject, predicate, and relation) or four (adding occurrence/non-occurrence). The commentator clarifies the ancient doctrine and refutes a potential misunderstanding using examples from the Quran and grammatical principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection claims that if the object of assent is the relation itself, then the proposition would have only three parts, but that doesn't clearly indicate the author's choice of the ancient doctrine because one could interpret 'assent' as implicitly including 'assent to occurrence' or 'assent to non-occurrence'.
2
Understand the commentator's clarification
The commentator explains that the author will later explicitly affirm the tripartite division, so the objection is baseless and the apparent meaning is sufficient.
3
Recognize the role of ellipsis (taqdir)
The opponent suggests that 'assent' might be elliptical, meaning 'assent to occurrence' or 'assent to non-occurrence'. The commentator refutes this by noting that such interpretation is a distortion (ta'wil) when the clear wording points to the relation itself.
Quranic examples like 'to protect you from the heat' implicitly meaning 'and from the cold' are analogies, but the commentator argues they do not apply here.
4
Distinguish three vs. four parts
Grasp that occurrence/non-occurrence is not an additional part because the relation already entails either occurrence or non-occurrence. The proposition is fully captured by subject, predicate, and the relation (affirmative or negative).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اذن فما معنى قول الشارح و اختار مذهب القدماء حيث جعل متعلق الاذعان هو النسبة الخبرية الثبوتية أو السلبية لا وقوع النسبة او لا وقوعها ؟ و الوقوع واللا وقوع لا يصير القضية اربعة اجزاء موضوعاً و محمولاً و نسبة و وقوعاً او لا وقوعاً بعد ان عرفت ان الوقوع واللا وقوع ليسا امراً زائداً على نفس النسبة (التقريب ص ۱۵-۱۶) (۳۱) قوله و سيشير المصنف: دفع لما ربما يتوهم من ان جعل متعلق الاذعان و الحكم نفس النسبة لا وقوعها او لا وقوعها ظاهراً لا يدل على اختياره مذهب المتقدمين الجواز ان يقدر مضاف و معطوف في الكلام اى العلم ان كان اذعاناً لوقوع النسبة أولا وقوعها، وكلاهما جايز واقع في الفصيح، اما الاول فظاهر واما الثانى فقد ذكره جماعة من النحويين ومثلواله بامثلة منها قوله تعالى: «... وجعل لكم سرابيل تقيكم الحرّ...» (سورة النحل الآية (۸۱ اى و البرد ومنها قوله تعالى: (فذكر ان نفعت الذكرى» (سورة الاعلى الآية(٩ :اى و ان لم تنفع. و حاصل :الدفع انه سيشير الى تثليث اجزاء القضية الذى هو مذهب القدماء فحينئذ لا جواز لما ذكر لكونه تفسيراً بما لا يرضى صاحبه و احتمال تغيير مذهبه او مراعاته لمذهب الغير بعيد جداً مع ان ظواهر الالفاظ حجة و احتمال التقدير تأويل. (محمد علی)
So what is the meaning of the commentator's statement: 'He chose the doctrine of the ancients by making the object of assent (idh'an) the predicative relation, whether affirmative or negative, not the occurrence of the relation or its non-occurrence?' And occurrence and non-occurrence do not make the proposition have four parts: subject, predicate, relation, and occurrence or non-occurrence, after you have known that occurrence and non-occurrence are not something additional over the relation itself. (al-Taqrib pp. 15-16) (31) His statement: 'And the author will indicate': a refutation of what might be imagined, that making the object of assent and judgment the relation itself, not its occurrence or non-occurrence, apparently does not indicate his choice of the doctrine of the Ancients, because it is permissible to assume an omitted noun and a conjunction in the speech, i.e., knowledge if it is assent to the occurrence of the relation or to its non-occurrence; both are permissible and occur in eloquent speech. As for the first, it is apparent. As for the second, a group of grammarians have mentioned it and exemplified it with examples, including His statement, the Exalted: '... and He made for you garments to protect you from the heat...' (Surat al-Nahl, verse 81), i.e., and from the cold. And among them is His statement, the Exalted: 'So remind, if the reminder benefits' (Surat al-A'la, verse 9), i.e., and if it does not benefit. The summary of the refutation: that the author will indicate the tripartite division of the proposition, which is the doctrine of the Ancients; then there is no justification for what was mentioned, because it is an interpretation that does not satisfy its proponent. And the possibility of his changing his doctrine or taking into account the doctrine of others is very remote, especially since the apparent meanings of words are proof, and the possibility of assumption is a distortion.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) where Muhammad Ali, the commentator, refutes a possible misunderstanding regarding the parts of a proposition and the author's choice of the ancient doctrine. The objection raised is: If the author makes the object of assent the predicative relation itself rather than its occurrence or non-occurrence, then the proposition would have only three parts (subject, predicate, relation), not four. However, the commentator explains that the author will later indicate the tripartite division, which aligns with the ancient doctrine. The objection that this does not clearly indicate the author's choice is countered by noting that it is permissible to understand 'assent' as including 'assent to occurrence' or 'assent to non-occurrence' through ellipsis (taqdir). The commentator provides examples from the Quran where an omitted phrase is understood, such as 'garments to protect you from the heat' meaning also 'from the cold'. The refutation concludes that the author's later statement will confirm the tripartite division, and any interpretation that suggests otherwise is a distortion (ta'wil) unsupported by the apparent meaning.

النسبة الخبرية
تثليث أجزاء القضية

Key Takeaway
The proposition consists of three parts: subject, predicate, and the predicative relation; occurrence/non-occurrence is not an additional part but is inherent in the relation.

Refuting the Claim of a Non-Productive Proposition
Author refutes commentator's example of non-productive proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the claim that a self-evident proposition cannot serve as a productive premise, using the author's syllogistic demonstration.

Core Idea
Even the most obvious proposition can be a premise for new knowledge.

The commentator argued that 'fire is hot' is an example of assented information that does not lead to unknown knowledge. The author of this hashiya corrects this, showing that any assented proposition, regardless of self-evidence, can be part of a syllogism yielding new information. In this lesson, we trace the author's argument and see how a simple self-evident premise generates a novel conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the commentator's claim
The commentator asserted that the proposition 'fire is hot' is an example of assented information that does not lead to an unknown. Locate this claim in the passage.
2
Understand the author's distinction
The author distinguishes between conceptual partial information (which may lack capacity to lead) and assented propositions (which always have capacity because they are complete relations). Note that the author argues the latter always can lead to unknowns.
3
Examine the syllogism with 'fire is hot'
The author constructs a categorical syllogism: 'Fire is hot' (minor premise), 'Everything hot has such-and-such effect' (major premise), thus 'Fire has such-and-such effect' (conclusion). This yields a new effect previously unknown.
The author uses a placeholder 'such-and-such effect' to indicate any unknown property.
4
Dismiss the condition of profoundness
The author rejects the idea that a premise must be weighty or profound to be productive. Even a trivial self-evident proposition can be a path to new knowledge.
5
Generalize the principle
The author concludes that all assented propositions, no matter how obvious, have the capacity to lead to unknowns. This refutes the commentator's specific example and the underlying assumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شأنية الايصال الى المجهولات فالمعلومات الجزئية التصورية يصدق عليها انها معلومة ولكن الجزئيتها فاقدة لشأنية الايصال واما المعلومات التصديقية فيا انها نسب تامة وقضايا قائمة بمعان مستقلة لا يتصور فيها عدم الشأنية في الايصال الى المجهولات ولوكانت نتائج هذا الايصال طقيفة بديهية فان كلامنا فيا هو موصل وليس بموصل لا انه ضخم المعنى او عاديه وتمثيل الشارع لمالايوصل من المعلومات التصديقية بقوله: النار حارة، غلط فان هذه القضية معلوم تصديقى يوصل الى مجهول تصديقى فيقال: النار حارة وكل حار فهو ذو كذا اثر فالنار ذات كذا اثر فنستفيد اثراً للنار لم نكن نعرفه لها ولا يضر معلومنا التصديق هذا النار حارة أنه بديهي للغاية فأننا شأن جميع عقلاء العالم لم تشترط في المعلوم الذي هو طريق للاتصال بالمجهول ان يكون موقوراً متيناً تلحظه الافكار والانظار باحترام واكرام ولسنا نستهدف بكلامنا هذا قضية النار حارة وحدها بل كافة مالها من نظير
The capacity to lead to unknowns: partial conceptual information is indeed known, but its particularity lacks the capacity to lead [to unknowns]. As for assented information, it consists of complete relations and propositions standing on independent meanings, in which lack of capacity to lead to unknowns is inconceivable, even if the results of this leading are trivial and self-evident. For our discussion concerns what does lead and what does not lead—not whether its meaning is great or ordinary. The commentator's example of what does not lead among assented information by his saying: 'Fire is hot,' is a mistake. For this proposition is an assented known that leads to an assented unknown. For it is said: 'Fire is hot, and everything hot has such and such effect, therefore fire has such and such effect.' Thus we derive an effect of fire that we did not know for it. Nor does it harm that our assented known 'fire is hot' is extremely self-evident. For the condition for the known, which is a path to connection with the unknown, is that it be weighty and solid, observed by thoughts and views with respect and honor—this is not our goal. We do not target by this speech the proposition 'fire is hot' alone, but all its counterparts.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement where the author (the hashiya writer) corrects the commentator (al-sharih) on the example of a proposition that allegedly does not lead to unknown knowledge. The commentator had claimed that the proposition 'fire is hot' is an example of assented information that does not yield new knowledge. The author refutes this by showing that even a self-evident proposition like 'fire is hot' can serve as a premise in a syllogism to deduce a previously unknown effect of fire. The author explains that assented information always has the capacity to lead to unknowns because it consists of complete relations and independent propositions, unlike conceptual partial information which may lack this capacity due to its particularity. The author also dismisses the objection that the premise must be weighty or profound; any known premise, however self-evident, can be a path to new knowledge. The argument is rooted in the very nature of assented propositions: they are complete truths that can be combined with other truths to produce conclusions. The author uses a simple syllogistic form: 'Fire is hot; everything hot has effect X; therefore fire has effect X.' This yields a new piece of information about fire. The commentator's mistake was to assume that only non-self-evident propositions are productive.

معلوم تصديقي
بديهي

D) Because the commentator used a conceptual known instead of an assented known.

Key Takeaway
Self-evident propositions can be productive premises in syllogisms; the commentator's mistake was assuming otherwise.

The extra condition for التزام signification
Explains that التزام needs a necessary connection while the other two only need knowledge of convention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the condition required for al-iltizam from the conditions required for al-mutabaqa and al-tadammun, and explain why the extra condition is necessary.

Core Idea
Signification by التزام needs a real connection; otherwise, any external would be signified, leading to absurdity.

In this passage, the commentator explains why the third type of signification—implication (التزام)—cannot be automatically assumed from mere knowledge of the linguistic convention, unlike the primary (مطابقة) and inclusive (تضمن) significations. Without a necessary link between the meaning and the implied external thing, a word would imply everything, which is impossible. Understanding this distinction is crucial for precise semantic analysis in Islamic legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of signification
Recall that الدلالة الوضعية includes مطابقة (word and whole meaning), تضمن (word and part of meaning), and التزام (word and something external to the meaning). In this passage, the author argues that the first two are conditional only on knowledge of the convention.
2
Understand the problem of infinite signification
The author argues that if التزام had no extra condition, a single word would potentially signify every external thing related to its meaning, leading to infinite significations. This is absurd because obviously words have limited significations.
3
Locate the extra condition for التزام
The condition is a necessary connection (لزوم) between the original meaning and the external thing signified. This connection must be real, not merely mental or conventional.
4
Contrast with مطابقة and تضمن
For these two, knowledge of the وضع (convention) is sufficient. The word brings to mind its meaning(s) or parts immediately upon hearing, regardless of speaker intent. The author emphasizes that signification does not depend on قصد (speaker's intent).
5
Apply the distinction to an Islamic example
Consider the term 'amr (command) in usul al-fiqh. By مطابقة it signifies a command. By تضمن it signifies the utterance itself. By التزام it may signify obligation if there is a necessary connection, as argued by some scholars. This distinction affects whether obligation is considered a direct or implied meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله في الدلالة الالتزامية لما كان الالتزام عبارة عن دلالة اللفظ على الخارج ولا ريب في ان اللفظ لا يدل على كل امر خارج و الالزم ان يدل لفظ واحد على معان غير متناهية لعدم التفاوت بينها فلا بدلها من شرط آخر بخلاف المطابقة والتضمن لظهور انه يكفي فيها العلم بالوضع من غير اشتراط بشيء آخر اما الاولى فظاهر و أما الثانية فكذلك ايضاً فان فهم الجزء لازم لفهم الكل وهكذا اذا كان اللفظ موضوعاً لمعان متعددة بوضع عليحدة او اذا كان للموضوع له الواحد اجزاء متعددة فانه يفهم منه العلم بالوضع عند اطلاقه جميع هذه المعانى او تلك الاجزاء فيكون دالاً على كل واحد من هذه المعاني مطابقة وعلى كل واحد من تلك الاجزاء تضمناً و ان لم يعلم ان مراد المتكلم ماذا من بين هذه المعانى او تلك الاجزاء ، فان الدلالة ليست بموقوفة على الارادة لظهور ان المعانى انما تفهم من الالفاظ عند الاطلاق و ان لم تكن مرادة للمتكلم وليس لنا لفظ واحد موضوع لكل واحد من معان غير متناهية اوضاع غير متناهية ولا لفظ واحد موضوع لمعنى مركب من اجزاء غير متناهية حتى يلزم دلالة اللفظ على الامور الغير المتناهية دلالة مطابقية او تضمنية فيلزم اشتراط أمر آخر لتحقق الدلالة فيهما كما في الالتزام.
His statement regarding implicational signification: Since implication is the signification of the word to an external thing, and there is no doubt that the word does not signify every external thing—otherwise a single word would signify infinite meanings, there being no distinction among them—it must have another condition, unlike مطابقة and تضمن, because it is obvious that knowledge of the linguistic convention suffices for them without needing any other condition. As for the first, it is apparent; and as for the second, likewise, because understanding the part is necessary for understanding the whole. Thus, if a word is assigned to multiple meanings by separate conventions, or if the single assigned meaning has multiple parts, then from the knowledge of the convention when it is uttered, all these meanings or those parts are understood, so it signifies each of these meanings by مطابقة and each of those parts by تضمن, even if one does not know what the speaker intends among these meanings or parts. For signification is not dependent on intent, because it is apparent that meanings are understood from words when uttered even if they are not intended by the speaker. And we do not have a single word assigned to each of infinite meanings by infinite conventions, nor a single word assigned to a meaning composed of infinite parts, such that the word would signify infinite things by مطابقة or تضمن, thus requiring another condition for their actualization as in the case of التزام.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage argues that التزام needs an extra condition (لزوم) to limit the externals signified; otherwise, any external would be signified, leading to infinitude. For مطابقة and تضمن, knowledge of the convention suffices: the word immediately brings to mind its meaning(s) or parts, even if multiple, and even if the speaker does not intend them. The author emphasizes that signification is not dependent on the speaker's intent. Thus, only التزام requires an additional restriction, namely a real necessary connection between the meaning and the external thing.

Key Terms
الوضع
الارادة

Checkpoint
Why does التزام need an extra condition?
A) Because it depends on speaker intent.
B) To avoid infinite signification.
C) Because it is a weaker form.
D) Because it only applies to composites.

Key Takeaway
التزام requires a real necessary connection (لزوم) to avoid the absurdity of a word signifying all things related to its meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

By تضمن it means a part like bowing. By التزام it might imply fear of Allah? Considering the necessary connection, explain which significations require an extra condition.
?
Explain why the word 'zina' (adultery) does not automatically signify 'punishment' by التزام unless the necessary connection (لزوم) is established, and contrast this with how 'zina' signifies by مطابقة and تضمن.

Rational vs. Conventional Necessity in Implication
Clarifies that the necessary connection for التزام can be rational or conventional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish rational necessity from conventional necessity and identify their roles in understanding التزام in Islamic texts.

Core Idea
Implication can be necessary by reason or by common usage; both are valid for التزام.

In Islamic logic, the relation between a concomitant (اللازم) and its necessary concomitant (الملزوم) can be of two kinds: rational necessity, where the mind cannot separate them, and conventional necessity, where common linguistic usage creates the link. This lesson unpacks both types, using the famous example of 'blindness' as a case study. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting implicit meanings in the Quran and other Islamic discourses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of necessity
Read the first line: 'ولابد من اللزوم عقلاً'. The author states that the necessary connection (لزوم) can be rational. The passage then implies a second type, conventional necessity, through the discussion of common usage. Note the contrast: rational necessity is strict mental impossibility; conventional necessity is based on widespread linguistic practice.
The term 'العرفي' is not explicit in the Arabic but is deduced from the context.
2
Analyze the definition of blindness
Blindness is defined as 'العدم المقيد بالبصر' (the negation restricted by sight). Rationally, blindness is the absence of sight, so it necessarily relates to sight. This is rational necessity: you cannot conceive of blindness without conceiving of the sight that is absent.
3
Examine the Quranic usage
The Quran attributes blindness to eyes (e.g., 'عميت ابصارهم' - their eyes are blind). Rationally, eyes are the organ of sight, but blindness is the negation of sight, not a property of the eyes themselves. Yet this attribution is common and accepted without a figurative indicator (قرينة مجازية). This shows conventional necessity at work: language usage makes the connection, even if not rationally necessary.
The author notes that such attributions are widespread in Arabic, so the example is valid.
4
Distinguish rational vs. conventional
Summarize: In rational necessity, the implication holds because the mind cannot separate the two concepts. In conventional necessity, the implication holds because speakers of the language have made the connection habitual. Both are valid for التزام in Islamic logic.
This distinction is important for interpreting ambiguous texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ولابد من اللزوم عقلاً بان يمتنع عقلاً تصور الملزوم بدون تصور اللازم كما بين العمى والبصر فان العمى موضوع للعدم المقيد بالبصر والبصر خارج عنه فان اسناده إلى البصر شايع بدون قرينة مجازية. قال الله تعالى: «فانها لا تعمى الابصار ولكن تعمى القلوب التي في الصدور»، ( سورة الحج الايه (۴۶) و قال تعالى: «عميت ابصارهم» الى غير ذلك من النظائر الشائعة والاصل الحقيقة على أن المناقشة فى المثال غیر رضی . جلال الدین)
His statement: 'And necessity is required rationally' meaning that it is rationally impossible to conceive the concomitant without conceiving the necessary concomitant, as between blindness and sight. For blindness is assigned to the negation restricted by sight, and sight is external to it, and its attribution to sight is common without a figurative context. Allah says: 'For indeed it is not the eyes that are blind, but it is the hearts within the breasts that are blind' (al-Hajj 46), and He says: 'Their eyes are blind' and other such common parallels. And the principle is literalness, so that the criticism of the example is not satisfactory. Jalal al-Din.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage explains that the لزوم (necessary connection) required for التزام can be either rational or conventional. Rational necessity means the mind cannot separate the concomitant from the necessary concomitant, as blindness (absence of sight) cannot be conceived without sight. However, conventional necessity is shown by common usage: in the Quran, blindness is attributed to eyes even though rationally blindness is not a property of eyes but the absence of sight. This attribution is widespread without a figurative indicator, proving that conventional (عرفي) necessity suffices for التزام.

Key Terms
اللزوم العقلي
اللزوم العرفي
العمى

Checkpoint

A) That rational necessity is required for التزام.
B) That conventional necessity is sufficient for التزام.
C) That blindness is a property of eyes.
D) That the Quran always uses figurative language.

Key Takeaway
Implication can be necessary either by reason or by convention; both are valid for understanding التزام in Islamic discourse.
