How Blindness Signifies Sight: Implication vs. Inclusion
Defends that blindness signifies sight by implication, not inclusion, by analyzing the concept of blindness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the concept of 'adam muḍāf (relative negation) to determine whether a term signifies its correlative by implication (iltizām) or inclusion (taḍammun).

Core Idea
Blindness signifies sight not by inclusion but by implication, because sight is external to the concept of negation.

In this lesson, we examine a key distinction in logical semantics: when a term like 'blindness' involves the absence (al-‘adam) of sight, is sight part of the concept or merely associated by implication? The commentator defends the latter, using the analysis of 'al-‘adam al-muḍāf' (the relative negation). We will walk through the logical structure of such concepts and see how Quranic evidence supports the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key objection
The objection claims that since blindness implies sight, sight must be part of its concept (taḍammun). Understand why this would make the relation inclusive.
2
Analyze the structure of 'al-‘adam al-muḍāf'
Blindness is defined as the negation added to sight (al-‘adam al-muḍāf ilā al-baṣar). The addition (iḍāfa) is part of the concept, but the object of addition (sight) remains external.
The ‘adam is a relative negation; its concept includes the relation but not the relatum.
3
Apply the distinction to the signification
Because sight is external to the concept, the connection is merely implicational (iltizām), not inclusive (taḍammun). The concept requires conceiving of sight, but sight is not a constituent.
4
Test with Quranic evidence
Examine how blindness is attributed to eyes in the Quran. If sight were included, this attribution would be improper. The fact that it is used directly shows the restriction is also external, indicating absolute negation—but that is false, so the attribution itself is the indicator (qarīna) that the signification is conventional.
The very attribution to eyes serves as evidence that sight is not part of the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله كالبصر بالنسبة الى العمى : لايقال : البصر جزء مفهوم العمى فلا تكون دلالته عليه بالالتزام بل بالتضمن . لا نا نقول : العمي عدم البصر اعنى: العدم المضاف الى البصر ، لا العدم والبصر، والمضاف اذا اخذ من حيث انه مضاف كانت الاضافة داخلة فيه والمضاف اليه خارجاً عنه وان كان مفهوم العمى هو العدم المضاف الى البصر، كانت الاضافة الى البصر داخلة في مفهومه والمضاف اليه خارجاً عنه فتعقل العدم من حيث أنه مضاف الى البصر، لا يكون بدون تعقل البصر و ان كان البصر خارجاً عن مفهومه. ثم انه اذا كان مفهومه عدم البصر، أى : العدم المقيد بالبصر، فلا يصح اسناده اليه وقد قال الله تعالى: «فانها لا تعمى الابصار و لكن تعمى القلوب التى فى الصدور و قال ايضاً عميت ابصارهم» بدون قرينة على ان المراد بالعمى هو العدم المطلق، هكذا قيل. وفيه انه يدل على ان التقييد بالبصر ايضاً خارج عن مفهوم العمى لانه لوكان داخلاً فيه لم يصح اسناده إلى البصر بدون قرينة فلزم ان يكون العمى عبارة عن مطلق العدم و هو باطل على ان هيهنا قرينة و هى نفس اسناده إلى البصر. (عبد الرحيم)
His statement: 'Like sight in relation to blindness': It should not be said that sight is part of the concept of blindness, so its signification to it is not by التزام but by تضمن. Because we say: Blindness is the absence of sight, i.e., the negation added to sight, not the negation and sight. When the added thing is taken from the aspect that it is added, the addition is included in it and the object of addition is external to it. So if the concept of blindness is the negation added to sight, then the addition to sight is included in its concept and the object of addition is external to it. Thus, conceiving the negation from the aspect that it is added to sight is not without conceiving sight, even though sight is external to its concept. Then, if its concept is the absence of sight, i.e., the negation restricted by sight, then its attribution to sight is not valid. And Allah has said: 'For indeed it is not the eyes that are blind, but it is the hearts that are blind' and also 'Their eyes are blind' without a figurative indicator indicating that what is meant by blindness is absolute negation. This is what is said. And in it is an indication that the restriction by sight is also external to the concept of blindness, because if it were internal, its attribution to eyes would not be valid without a figurative indicator. Then it would follow that blindness is absolute negation, which is false. Moreover, there is here an indicator, which is the very attribution to eyes. (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage refutes the objection that sight is part of the concept of blindness, which would make the signification تضمن rather than التزام. The commentator argues that blindness is the negation added to sight (العدم المضاف إلى البصر), not the negation plus sight. The addition (إضافة) is included in the concept, but the object of addition (المضاف إليه, i.e., sight) is external. Therefore, conceiving blindness requires conceiving sight, but sight is not part of the concept. Thus, the relation is implicational, not inclusive. The Quranic verses are cited to show that blindness is attributed to eyes, which would be invalid if blindness were the negation restricted by sight (i.e., including the restriction) because then it could not be attributed to sight without a figurative indicator. This proves that the restriction is also external to the concept, meaning blindness is absolute negation, but that is false. The very attribution serves as an indicator that the connection is conventional.

Key Terms
العدم المضاف
الالتزام
التضمن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the signification of blindness to sight considered التزام not تضمن?
A) Because sight is part of the concept of blindness.
B) Because blindness is the negation added to sight, and sight is external to the concept.
C) Because blindness is absolute negation.
D) Because the Quran uses figurative language.

Key Takeaway
The concept of a relative negation includes the addition but excludes the object, so the signification of the object is by implication, not inclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the concept of 'knowledge' (al-‘ilm) as the negation of ignorance? Using the same method, determine whether 'ignorance' is part of the concept of knowledge by inclusion or implication. Provide your reasoning.
?
Identify a Quranic verse where a relational term is used (e.g., 'hearing' and 'deafness'), and analyze whether the connection to its correlative is by inclusion or implication.

Inclusion and Implication Presuppose Conformity Signification
Proves that تضمن and التزام depend on مطابقة because they require the word's assigned meaning first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why signification by inclusion (تضمن) and implication (التزام) necessarily presuppose signification by conformity (مطابقة).

Core Idea
Inclusion and implication cannot function without first understanding the core meaning (المسمى).

In the study of conventional signification (الدلالة الوضعية), three types are identified: conformity (مطابقة), inclusion (تضمن), and implication (التزام). This passage establishes that the latter two are dependent on the first. Understanding this hierarchy is crucial for analyzing how words convey meanings in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of conventional signification
Locate the three terms: مطابقة (conformity), تضمن (inclusion), and التزام (implication). These are the three divisions of conventional signification.
2
Understand the definitions
مطابقة signifies the whole named meaning; تضمن signifies a part of the named meaning; التزام signifies something external to the named meaning that is necessarily implied by it.
The passage defines them in relation to المسمى (the named thing).
3
Follow the reasoning chain
The passage argues: signification to a part (تضمن) is not realized without signification to the whole (مطابقة); similarly, signification to something external (التزام) is not realized without signification to the thing it is external to. Therefore, both depend on مطابقة.
The reasoning is based on the impossibility of understanding a part without the whole, or an external without the core.
4
Consider the two supporting arguments
The commentator mentions two lines of proof: (1) inclusion and implication require the linguistic convention (وضع), and the convention requires conformity; (2) a more direct argument that inclusion/implication cannot be understood without conformity.
The text cuts off before completing the second argument, but the idea is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله اذ لاشك ان الدلالة الوضعية على جزء المسمى ولازمه فرع الدلالة على المسمى : وذلك لان الدلالة التضمنية هى الدلالة على جزء المسمى و الدلالة الالتزامية هى الدلالة على خارج المسمى ولا ارتياب فى ان الدلالة على جزء المسمى من حيث انه جزء لا يتحقق بدون الدلالة على المسمى وكذلك الدلالة على الخارج عن المسمى من حيث انه خارج ، لا يتحقق بدون الدلالة عليه. قال بعض اهل الصنعة فى بيان استلزام التضمن والالتزام المطابقة ان التضمن والالتزام يستلزمان الوضع، والوضع يستلزم المطابقة، والتضمن والالتزام يستلزمان المطابقة. وقال بعضهم: ان التضمن
His statement: 'For there is no doubt that conventional signification to a part of the named thing and its necessary concomitant is a branch of signification to the named thing.' That is because signification by inclusion is signification to a part of the named thing, and signification by implication is signification to what is outside the named thing. And there is no doubt that signification to a part of the named thing from the aspect that it is a part is not realized without signification to the named thing, and likewise signification to what is outside the named thing from the aspect that it is outside is not realized without signification to it. Some people of the craft said in clarifying that inclusion and implication entail conformity: that inclusion and implication entail the linguistic convention (وضع), and the convention entails conformity, so inclusion and implication entail conformity. And some said that inclusion...
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage argues that both التضمن (inclusion) and التزام (implication) are dependent on مطابقة (conformity). Because inclusion signifies a part of the named thing, and implication signifies something external to the named thing, both cannot be understood without first understanding the named thing itself (المسمى). The commentator quotes two opinions on the matter: one says that inclusion and implication require the convention (وضع), and the convention requires conformity, so they entail conformity; another opinion is given but the text cuts off. This establishes the foundational role of مطابقة.

Key Terms
الدلالة الوضعية
المسمى

D) Because they are not conventional significations.

Key Takeaway
Inclusion and implication are branches of conformity; the whole meaning (المسمى) is primary in conventional signification.

Definition of Declarative Sentence and the Objection of Necessary Truths
Author defines khabar and defends its definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why necessarily true statements (e.g., 'the whole is greater than the part') still fit the logical definition of a declarative sentence as admitting possibility of truth and falsehood.

Core Idea
A declarative sentence must be able to be true or false in the mind, regardless of actual reality.

In logic, a declarative sentence (khabar) is defined as that which can be true or false. But what about statements that are necessarily true, like 'the whole is greater than the part'? The logician addresses this by distinguishing between the sentence considered in itself and its actual correspondence with reality. This definition and its key nuance are central to understanding propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of khabar
The author states that a khabar is defined as a complete sentence that is independent in giving information (istiqlal bi-l-ifada) and possibly admits truth and falsehood (ihtimal al-sidq wa al-kadhib). Locate this definition in the Arabic text.
The definition includes two elements: independence and possibility of truth/falsehood.
2
Understand the objection
An objector argues that statements like 'the whole is greater than the part' are necessarily true, so they do not 'possibly' admit falsehood. Similarly, 'the part is greater than the whole' is necessarily false and does not 'possibly' admit truth. This would make the definition incomplete (ghayr jami').
The objection targets the word 'possibly' (ihtimal).
3
Grasp the solution: logical possibility vs. actual reality
The author responds that the possibility is considered by merely looking at the sentence's concept (bi-mujarrad al-nazar ila mafhumihi), ignoring external occurrence (al-wuqu' al-khariji). So when considered in itself, the statement 'the sky is above us' can be conceived as true or false, even though it is actually true.
This is a distinction between logical possibility and actual truth.
4
Apply the same distinction to an Islamic science example
Consider the statement 'The world is temporally originated' (al-alam hadith). In kalam, this is argued as necessary. But as a khabar, if we consider the sentence in itself without assuming its truth, we can conceive it as possibly false. This shows it fits the definition.
The logical definition treats all khabar equally, regardless of epistemic certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(ميرزا محمد على)
i.e., it does not require the addition of another word to it in giving information, meaning it is independent in giving information, like 'Zayd is standing' for example, unlike 'Zayd' alone, for it requires in giving information the addition of something else to it like 'standing' or 'sitting' and their like, and likewise 'standing' alone. Then what is meant by 'silence' is the silence of the speaker, and it is possible the silence of the addressee and the silence of both, and each has a proponent. (44) His saying: 'if it possibly admits truth and falsehood': Let it not be said that our statement 'the whole is greater than the part' is absolutely true, not possibly false, and his statement 'the part is greater than the whole' is absolutely false, not possibly true, and likewise their like among statements whose truth or falsehood is absolute, like our saying 'the sky is above us and the earth is beneath us' or the opposite, so there would be a lack of comprehensiveness of the definition. For we say: What is meant is that it possibly admits them by merely considering its concept, without noting the particularity of the informant and the informed. So our saying 'the sky is above us' in itself possibly admits truth and falsehood, and likewise our saying 'the sky is beneath us', meaning that if you strip your consideration of external occurrence or non-occurrence and observe this statement in itself, it is possible in the mind for truth and falsehood.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage defines a declarative sentence (khabar) in logic. First, it explains that a declarative sentence is independent in giving information, unlike a single word like 'Zayd' which needs another word to form a complete sentence. The definition also mentions 'silence' as a condition, and the author notes that silence refers to the speaker's silence (after uttering the sentence). The main issue addressed is the definition of khabar as 'that which possibly admits truth and falsehood'. An objection is raised: some statements are necessarily true (like 'the whole is greater than the part') and some are necessarily false (like 'the part is greater than the whole'), so they do not 'possibly' admit truth and falsehood; they are fixed. The answer is that the possibility is considered with respect to the sentence itself, not the external reality. If we consider the sentence 'the sky is above us' in itself, without looking at whether it actually matches reality, the mind can conceive it as possibly true and possibly false. The definition is about the logical possibility, not the actual fact. This preserves the definition's comprehensiveness, including both necessary and contingent statements.

Classifying Non-Declarative Sentences
Classification of non-declarative sentences (insha) into types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the types of non-declarative sentences (insha') based on whether they indicate a request and the manner of request, and correctly classify given examples from Islamic texts.

Core Idea
Not all non-declaratives are requests; some merely notify.

In logic, sentences are either declarative (truth-bearing) or non-declarative (insha'). This passage from al-Yazdi's Hashiya provides a classification of insha' based on whether they conventionally indicate a request and, if so, what kind of request. Understanding this classification is essential for correctly interpreting Quranic verses and hadith, where amr and nahy carry specific normative force.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first division
The author divides insha' into two sets: those that do not conventionally indicate a request (called 'tanbih') and those that do. Tanbih includes expressions like hoping, calling, wondering, and oaths.
2
Distinguish request types by purpose
If the insha' indicates a request, then determine the intended purpose: either to obtain a concept in the mind (istifham/interrogation) or to obtain (or prevent) something in the external world.
Interrogation seeks a mental concept, not an action.
3
Analyze the manner of external request
For requests aimed at external reality, identify the social dynamic: superiority (amr/nahy), equality (iltimas), or humility (su'al). If superiority, further separate amr (requesting action) from nahy (requesting non-action).
Amr and nahy come from a superior, iltimas from an equal, su'al from an inferior.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم ان الانشاء اما ان لايدل على طلب الشيء بالوضع فهو «التنبيه» كالترجي والنداء و التعجب وغير ذلك كالقسم أو يدل فاما ان يكون المقصود حصول الشيء في الذهن من حيث هو كذلك فهو «الاستفهام» و اما ان المقصود حصول شيء في الخارج او عدم حصوله فيه بطريق الاستعلاء فهو «امر» إن كان المطلوب الفعل و «نهى» ان كان المطلوب عدم الفعل أو بطريق التساوى فهو «التماس» او بطريق الخضوع فهو ( السؤال » . (عبد الرحيم )
Know that the non-declarative (insha') either does not indicate a request for something by convention, and it is 'notification', like hoping, calling, wonder, and others, like oath; or it indicates. Then either the intended is the obtaining of the thing in the mind as such, and it is 'interrogation'; or the intended is the obtaining of something in the external world or its non-obtaining in it by way of superiority, and it is 'command' if the requested is an action, and 'prohibition' if the requested is non-action; or by way of equality, and it is 'request' (iltimas); or by way of humility, and it is 'supplication' (su'al).
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of non-declarative sentences (insha'), which are sentences that do not admit truth or falsehood. The author begins by dividing insha' into two main categories: those that do not conventionally indicate a request (like expressions of hope, calling, wonder, oaths) called 'tanbih' (notification), and those that do indicate a request. The latter are further subdivided based on the pragmatic purpose. If the purpose is to obtain the thing in the mind (i.e., a mental concept), it is 'istifham' (interrogation). If the purpose is to obtain or prevent something in the external world, it is either 'amr' (command) if the requested is an action, or 'nahy' (prohibition) if the requested is non-action. If the request is made on an equal footing (not with superiority), it is 'iltimas' (request), and if made with humility, it is 'su'al' (supplication). This classification is typical in logic and usul al-fiqh for understanding the meanings of language.

Key Takeaway
Non-declarative sentences are classified by whether they indicate a request, and if so, by the purpose (mental vs. external) and the manner of request (superiority, equality, humility).

Equality of Sleeping and Awake: Temporal Dispersion Allowed
Objection and answer about equality of 'sleeping' and 'awake' with temporal nuance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition for two universals to be equal (mutasāwiyān) and explain why temporal dispersion does not break equality.

Core Idea
Equality of universals requires coextensiveness, not simultaneity.

The objection that 'sleeping' and 'awake' cannot be equal because they are never true at the same time is common. But the logician's concept of equality is broader: it only requires that each universal applies to all individuals of the other, even if at different times. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector claims that 'sleeping' and 'awake' are equal yet lack universal truth from both sides. Recognize that the objector assumes equality requires simultaneous truth.
The objection is based on a misunderstanding of the temporal condition.
2
Extract the answer's core condition
The answer states that equality requires each universal to apply to all individuals of the other, but not at the same time. Focus on the phrase 'لا يلزم من ذلك ان يصدقا معاً في زمان واحد'.
3
Understand the temporal nuance
Sleeping applies to a person while asleep; awake applies to that same person when awake. Thus each applies to all individuals of the other across different times.
The author emphasizes that this is sufficient for equality.
4
Apply the principle to other relations
The author generalizes to absolute generality (عموم مطلقا) and generality from one side (من وجه). Consider how temporal dispersion affects other logical relations.
This shows the principle is not limited to equality but applies to truth conditions in general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(١٧) فان قلت: النائم والمستيقظ متساويان وليس بينهما صدق كلى من الجانبين. قلت المعتبر في المتساويين هو صدق كل واحد منهما على جميع افراد الاخر ولا يلزم من ذلك ان يصدقا معاً في زمان واحد فالمستيقظ والنائم يصدق كل واحد منهما على الاخر كلياً الا ان ذلك ليس في زمان واحد و ذلك لا يضر في كونهما متساويين. ومنهم من قال التساوى انما هو بين النائم فى الجملة والمستيقظ في الجملة والنائم في حال النوم يصدق عليه انه مستيقظ في الجملة و ان لم يصدق عليه انه مستيقظ في حال النوم وكذا المستيقظ يصدق عليه في حال تيقظه انه نائم في الجملة و المتساويان يصدق كل منهما على جميع افراد الآخر في زمان صدق الآخر عليه وقس على ذلك الصدق المعتبر في العموم والخصوص مطلقا و من وجه (شيخ عبد الرحيم)
(17) For you say: The sleeping and the awake are equal, but there is no universal truth between them from both sides. I say: What is considered in equals is that each one applies to all individuals of the other, and it does not necessitate that they apply together at one time. So the awake and the sleeping: each one applies to the other universally, except that this is not at one time, and that does not harm their being equal. Some said: Equality is only between the sleeping in general and the awake in general. The sleeping, while asleep, is described as being awake in general, even if he is not described as awake during sleep; similarly, the awake, while awake, is described as sleeping in general. The two equals: each applies to all individuals of the other at the time the other applies to it. And analogize to that the truth considered in absolute generality and particularity and from one side. (Shaykh Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection about the equality of the universals 'sleeping' and 'awake'. The objector claims they are equal (since every sleeping person is potentially awake and vice versa) but they are never true simultaneously of the same individual. The answer clarifies that equality does not require simultaneous predication; it only requires that each universal applies to all individuals of the other at some time. For example, at the time a person is asleep, 'sleeping' applies to him; at that time 'awake' does not apply, but 'awake' applies to him at another time. Thus each applies to all individuals of the other, albeit at different times. Some scholars add that 'sleeping in general' and 'awake in general' are equal, not the specific instances. The author emphasizes that this temporal flexibility applies similarly to the truth conditions for absolute generality (عموم مطلقا) and generality from one side (من وجه).

Key Takeaway
Equality of universals is based on coextensiveness across individuals, not on simultaneity of predication.

Reducing the Four Relations to Categorical Propositions
Author enumerates the standard propositional forms to which each of the four relations reduces.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct categorical propositional reduction for each of the four relations between universals.

Core Idea
Every relation between universals reduces to a specific pattern of categorical propositions.

The four relations — equality, absolute generality, generality from one side, and contrariety — are not merely abstract concepts. They can be expressed as precise combinations of universal and particular, affirmative and negative propositions. This lesson shows how each relation is captured by its marja' or reduction, including the often-overlooked modal aspect (perpetual vs. absolute).

Guided Path
1
Identify the four relations
Note the four relations discussed: tabaayun (contrariety), tasawi (equality), 'umum mutlaq (absolute generality), and 'umum min wajh (generality from one side). These are the standard relations between universals in logic.
2
Determine the quantity and quality for each
For each relation, the text specifies the quantity (universal or particular) and quality (affirmative or negative) of the propositions that express it. For example, contrariety yields two universal negatives.
3
Incorporate modality
The author also considers modality: whether the propositions are perpetual (da'ima) or absolute (mutlaqa). Affirmatives are absolute, negatives are perpetual, except for 'umum mutlaq where the negative is particular and absolute.
Perpetual means true at all times; absolute means true without necessity of modality.
4
Match each relation to its reduction
Combine the above: tabaayun → two universal perpetual negatives; tasawi → two universal absolute affirmatives; 'umum mutlaq → one universal absolute affirmative + one particular absolute negative; 'umum min wajh → one particular absolute affirmative + two particular perpetual negatives.
5
Understand the uniqueness of 'umum min wajh
The commentator explains why 'umum min wajh is not reduced to two particular affirmatives and a particular negative: that pattern matches 'umum mutlaq, so it would not distinguish the two relations. The addition of perpetual negatives ensures distinctness.
This step highlights the precision required in logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(١٩) قوله و مرجع التباين الى سالبتين: المرجع بكسر الجيم مصدر ميمى بمعنى الرجوع لا اسم مكان بمعنى موضع الرجوع وذلك بدليل تعديته بـ «الى» والمصدر الميمى يأتي على وزن مفعل بفتح العين من كل باب الإشاذاً كـ مرجع ومغفرة ومفازة و معذرة و معصية». واعلم ان ما ذكره المحشى من مرجع النسب الاربع انما هو بحسب الكمية والكيفية واما بحسب الجهة فرجع التباين الى سالبتين كليتين دائمتين، لان المباينة بين الكليين هي ان لا يتصادقا اصلاً سواء امكن ام لا ومرجع التساوى الى موجبتين كليتين مطلقتين عامتين لان المساواة بين الكليين ان يتصادقا بالفعل سواء وجب ذلك الصدق ام لا ومرجع العموم مطلقا الى موجبة كلية مطلقة عامة وسالبة جزئية لة ومرجع العموم من وجه الى موجبة جزئية مطلقة عامة وسالبتين جزئيتين دائمتين. فالموجبات مطلقة دائمة و السولب دائمة (عبد الرحيم) (٢٠) قوله ومرجع العموم من وجه .... فان قلت كما ان مرجع العموم الى ذلك، كذلك مرجعه إلى موجبتين جزئيتين وسالبة جزئية فلم ترك المحشى ذلك وتعرض لما ذكره ؟ قلت: لانه على هذا لا يتمايز عن العموم المطلق لان مرجعه ايضاً الى ذلك (عبد الرحيم)
(19) His saying: 'And the marja' of contrariety is to two negatives.' 'Marja'' with a kasra is a verbal noun meaning returning, not a noun of place meaning place of return, as evidenced by its being modified by 'ila'. The verbal noun comes on the pattern of maf'il with fatha on the 'ayn from every derived form, like marja', maghfira, mafaza, ma'dhara, and ma'siya. Know that what the commentator mentioned about the marja' of the four relations is only according to quantity and quality. As for modality, the marja' of contrariety (tabaayun) is two universal perpetual negatives, because contrariety between two universals is that they do not coincide at all, whether possible or not. The marja' of equality (tasawi) is two universal absolute general affirmatives, because equality between two universals is that they coincide in actuality, whether that truth is necessary or not. The marja' of absolute generality ('umum mutlaq) is a universal absolute general affirmative and a particular negative for it. The marja' of generality from one side ('umum min wajh) is a particular absolute general affirmative and two particular perpetual negatives. So the affirmatives are absolute, and the negatives are perpetual. (Abd al-Rahim) (20) His saying: 'And the marja' of 'umum min wajh ...' For you say: Just as the marja' of 'umum is that, similarly its marja' is two particular affirmatives and a particular negative; so why did the commentator leave that and mention what he mentioned? I say: Because on that it would not be distinguished from absolute generality, since its marja' is also that. (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage explains the reduction (marja') of each of the four relations between universals to specific categorical propositions, taking into account quantity, quality, and modality. First, the commentator reiterates the grammatical point that 'marja'' is a verbal noun. Then he explains the reductions: 1) Contrariety (tabaayun) reduces to two universal perpetual negatives (e.g., 'No A is B and no B is A'). 2) Equality (tasawi) reduces to two universal absolute general affirmatives (e.g., 'Every A is B and every B is A'). 3) Absolute generality ('umum mutlaq) reduces to one universal affirmative and one particular negative (e.g., 'Every A is B, some B is not A'). 4) Generality from one side ('umum min wajh) reduces to one particular affirmative and two particular perpetual negatives (e.g., 'Some A is B, some A is not B, some B is not A'). A question is then raised: why not reduce 'umum min wajh to two particular affirmatives and a particular negative? The answer is that such a reduction would be identical to that of absolute generality, so the distinct reduction (with two perpetual negatives) is necessary to differentiate them. This demonstrates the precision required in logical analysis.

مرجع
مطلقة دائمة

Key Takeaway
The four relations between universals are precisely reducible to categorical propositions with specific quantities, qualities, and modalities.

Objection on reducing universal quantifiers to particular propositions
Author presents an objection and answer about the reduction of universal quantifiers to particular propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dialectical exchange on quantifier reduction and explain why an alternative reduction would collapse the distinction between universal and absolute universality.

Core Idea
An incorrect reduction of universal quantifiers erases the line between universal and absolute.

This passage presents a dialectical exchange common in logical commentaries. The commentator had previously claimed that universal quantifiers reduce to specific particular propositions. An objector proposes an alternative reduction, but the commentator shows it would conflate universal and absolute universality. Understanding this subtlety sharpens your grasp of how quantifiers function in Islamic logic and its application in legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Understand the original claim
The commentator asserts that universal quantifiers reduce to particular propositions: a universal affirmative reduces to a particular affirmative, and a universal negative to a particular negative.
This is the reduction being defended.
2
Identify the objection
The objector asks: why not reduce them to two particular affirmatives and a particular negative? This seems like a plausible alternative.
The objection challenges the commentator's choice as arbitrary.
3
Analyze the commentator's response
The commentator replies that this alternative would make universal quantifiers indistinguishable from absolute universality, since absolute universality also reduces to the same set of particulars.
The key is preserving a logical distinction.
4
Evaluate the logical ground
Absolute universality (e.g., 'Man is rational' without quantifier) has a broader reference. The reduction must maintain the quantifier's logical force.
The reduction is not arbitrary but necessary for logical precision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان قلت كما ان مرجع العموم الى ذلك، كذلك مرجعه إلى موجبتين جزئيتين وسالبة جزئية فلم ترك المحشى ذلك وتعرض لما ذكره ؟
قلت: لانه على هذا لا يتمايز عن العموم المطلق لان مرجعه ايضاً الى ذلك (عبد الرحيم)
For you said: Just as the reference of universality is to that, similarly its reference is to two particular affirmatives and a particular negative, so why did the commentator neglect that and present what he mentioned? He said: Because on this it would not be distinguished from absolute universality, since its reference is also to that. (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange (فان قلت ... قلت) regarding the reduction of universal propositions. The objector argues that if, as claimed, the universal quantifier reduces to particular propositions, then it should also reduce to two particular affirmatives and a particular negative. So why did the commentator mention something else? The answer is that if it reduced to those, then it would not be distinct from absolute universality (العموم المطلق) because absolute universality also reduces to the same. Thus the commentator's choice preserves the distinction. The note 'عبد الرحيم' likely indicates the source of this comment.

Key Terms
مرجع العموم
العموم المطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) Because the commentator prefers a different reduction.

Key Takeaway
The reduction of universal quantifiers must be chosen carefully to preserve logical distinctions; an incorrect reduction can collapse universal and absolute universality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

2. Write a brief debate where one person argues for the alternative reduction (two particular affirmatives and a particular negative) and the other defends the original reduction, using the reasoning from this passage.

Classifying Verbal Sentences as Categorical: Objection and Answer
A commentator raises and answers an objection about the classification of verbal sentences into categorical or conditional

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the objection that verbal sentences like 'Zaydun daraba' are neither categorical nor conditional by transforming them into equivalent nominal categoricals.

Core Idea
In meaning, a verbal sentence is a categorical proposition.

This passage presents a dialectical objection: how can verbal sentences like 'Zaydun daraba' be considered categorical or conditional? They seem to lack the subject-predicate structure of categoricals and the connection/disjunction of conditionals. The commentator answers by showing the verbal sentence is equivalent in meaning to a nominal categorical, preserving the subject-predicate relationship. This lesson unpacks the objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection claims that verbal sentences (e.g., 'Zaydun daraba') cannot be categorical or conditional because they lack a mubtada-khabar structure (for categorical) or connection/disjunction (for conditional). Notice how the objector focuses on the grammatical form rather than logical meaning.
The objector is assuming that only nominal sentences qualify as categorical.
2
Analyze the first answer: equivalence in meaning
The commentator argues that 'Zaydun daraba' is equivalent in meaning to 'Zaydun daribun' (Zayd is striking). This transforms the verbal sentence into a nominal categorical with subject 'Zayd' and predicate 'daribun'. The judgment becomes affirmation of a predicate for a subject.
This equivalence relies on the fact that the agent (fa'il) functions as the subject logically.
3
Understand the second objection
A related objection: even if transformed, the original sentence 'Zaydun daraba' does not explicitly have the form of affirmation/negation or connection/disjunction. It seems to escape the division entirely.
This objection is more fundamental, attacking the completeness of the division.
4
Grasp the commentator's broader answer
The commentator points out that the original matn used the phrase 'thubutu shay'in li shay'in' (affirmation of something for something), which is broader than 'haml' (predication). This phrase includes the relation in verbal sentences because they also affirm an action of an agent. So the division is complete.
Thus the objection arises only if one narrows the definition to predication alone.
5
Evaluate the debate
The commentator successfully defends the classification by showing that verbal sentences are reducible to categoricals in meaning and that the matn's wording was already inclusive. This is a typical 'expansion probe' in the sharh tradition.
No further objection is raised, indicating the answer is accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال بعض الشارحين فان قلت ان الوجه المذكور في التسمية انما يظهر فيها كان المحكوم عليه مبتداء و المحكوم به خبراً و اما فيما كان المحكوم عليه فاعلا و المحكوم به فعلاً فلا . قلت ان قولنا ضرب زيد فى قوة قولنا زيد ضارب فيكون قولنا «زید» موضوعاً و قولنا: (ضرب) محمولاً بحسب المأل. ثم قال: وبهذا ينكشف الجواب عماربما يتوهم من ان حصر القضية على الحملية والشرطية غير صحيح فان نحو ضرب زيد ليس بجملية ولا شرطية فان الحكم فيه ليس بالثبوت والنفى حتى يكون حملية ولا بالاتصال او الانفصال حتى يكون شرطية. لشيء و حاصل الجواب ان قولنا: ضرب زيد» في قوة زيد «ضارب فيكون الحكم فيه بثبوت شيء ء كما هو الواقع في الحمليات الموجبة. ولا يخفى ان هذا السؤال لا يرد على عبارة المصنف حتى يحتاج الى التقصى عنه فان ثبوت شيء لشيء اعم من ان يكون بطريق الحمل اولا بل انما يرد لوقيل: فان كان الحكم فيها بحمل شيء على شيء او نفيه عنه» فتأمل.
Some commentator said: If you say that the mentioned aspect in naming only appears when the subject is mubtada and the predicate is khabar, but when the subject is fa'il and the predicate is fi'l, it does not. I say: Our saying 'Zaydun daraba' is equivalent to our saying 'Zaydun daribun', so our saying 'Zayd' is subject and our saying 'daraba' is predicate in meaning. Then he said: And by this the answer is revealed to what is suspected that restricting the proposition to categorical and conditional is incorrect, because 'Zaydun daraba' is neither categorical nor conditional, since the judgment in it is not by affirmation and negation so that it be categorical, nor by connection or disconnection so that it be conditional. And the summary of the answer is that our saying 'Zaydun daraba' is equivalent to 'Zaydun daribun', so the judgment in it is about affirming something of something as is the case in positive categoricals. And it is not hidden that this question does not apply to the wording of the author such that it needs investigation, for 'the affirmation of something for something' is more general than being by way of predication or not. Rather it only applies if it were said: 'then the judgment in it is by predicating something of something or negating it of it.' So ponder.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical objection and answer about the classification of propositions. The objection is that the naming of 'subject' (mahkum alayh) and 'predicate' (mahkum bih) only works in nominal sentences (mubtada and khabar), not in verbal sentences like 'Zaydun daraba' (Zayd struck) where the subject is a fa'il (agent) and the predicate is a fi'l (verb). The commentator answers that 'Zaydun daraba' is equivalent in meaning to 'Zaydun daribun' (Zayd is striking), so in meaning it has a subject (Zayd) and a predicate (daribun). Hence it is a categorical proposition.

Then a further objection is raised: if propositions are restricted to categorical and conditional, verbal sentences like 'Zaydun daraba' seem to be neither because they don't have the explicit form of affirmation/negation nor connection/disjunction. The answer is again that such sentences are equivalent to categorical forms (e.g., 'Zayd is striking'). The commentator then notes that this objection does not apply to the original author's wording because he used the phrase 'thubutu shay'in li shay'in' (affirmation of something for something), which is broader than just predication; it includes even the relation in verbal sentences. So the objection only arises if one restricts the definition to 'haml' (predication).

This passage shows how later commentators refined the matn's taxonomy by showing that verbal sentences can be reduced to categorical form, and that the original matn's phrasing was already general enough to avoid the objection.

Key Terms
قضية حملية (qadiyya hamliyya): A categorical proposition where a predicate is affirmed or denied of a subject.
قضية شرطية (qadiyya shartiyya): A conditional or disjunctive proposition.
محكوم عليه (mahkum 'alayh): The subject of judgment, in a broad sense including both mubtada and fa'il.

B) Because it is equivalent in meaning to 'Zaydun daribun', which has a subject and predicate.
C) Because all Arabic sentences are categorical by default.
D) Because the verb 'daraba' implies a predicate.

Key Takeaway
Verbal sentences in Arabic are logically categorical because they are equivalent in meaning to nominal sentences with explicit subject and predicate.

Tripartite and Bipartite Propositions
Author discusses the classification of propositions into tripartite and bipartite based on presence of copula

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tripartite and bipartite propositions according to both the standard word-copula view and the alternative vowel-copula view, and apply each view to classify Islamic science propositions.

Core Idea
A proposition's structure depends on what you count as the copula: a word or a vowel.

The standard division of propositions into tripartite (with an explicit copula word like 'huwa') and bipartite (without such a word) seems straightforward. However, this passage reveals a deeper nuance: some logicians consider the copula to be the rafa' vowel itself. This shifts how we classify sentences like 'Zaydun qa'imun' and forces us to reconsider what counts as a 'part' of a proposition. By working through this passage, you will learn to identify which theory is being used and why it matters for logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of the standard classification
Find the description of tripartite and bipartite propositions. The standard view says: tripartite has three parts (subject, predicate, copula); bipartite has two parts (subject and predicate, without copula).
2
Locate the objection to the naming of 'bipartite'
The author notes that if the copula is omitted, the proposition might be considered as having only one part. He then offers two justifications: partial appropriateness or naming after the majority of cases.
3
Distinguish the two theories of the copula
Contrast the standard view where the copula is a word like 'huwa' with the alternative view where the copula is the rafa' vowel. Under the vowel view, inflected propositions (mu'rabat) become tripartite even without an explicit word.
Example: 'Zaydun qa'imun' is tripartite under the vowel view because the rafa' vowel acts as copula.
4
Apply both views to a new proposition from Islamic sciences
Take an Islamic proposition, e.g., 'al-‘alamu hadithun' (the world is temporally originated). Classify it under both theories. Under word-copula, it is bipartite; under vowel-copula, it is tripartite because 'al-‘alamu' and 'hadithun' have rafa' vowels.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله والقضية على الاول تسمى ثلاثية وعلى الثاني ثنائية اما الاول فلاشتمالها في اللفظ على ثلاثة اجزاء المحكوم عليه والمحكوم به والرابطة . و اما الثاني فلاشتمالها على جزئين منها : المحكوم عليه والمحكوم به هذا هو المشهور عند الجمهور. و فيه ان هاتين الصورتين اعنى صورة ان تذكر الرابطة وصورة ان تحذف اما ان تكونا مع ذكر الطرفين أو مع حذف احدهما و وجه التسمية على الاول ظاهر واما على الثاني فلا، فان القضية ح كانت مع ذكر الرابطة فتشتمل على جزئين او مع حذفها فعلى جزء واحد. اللهم الا ان يقال: أنه يكفى في التسمية وجود المناسبة فى البعض أو يقال ان الاغلب هو ذكر الطرفين فسمى الشيء باسم اغلب الأفراد. ثم لا يخفى ان التقسيم الثلاثية والثنائية على ما ذكر، انما هو عند من يجعل «هو» و «هي» و نحوهما روابط و اما عند من يقول : ان الرابطة حركة الرفع من الحركات الاعرابية وما يجرى مجربها فهو يقول: ان التركيب ان كان من المعربات فالقضية ثلاثية كقولنا : «زيد قائم» و ان كان من المبنيات فهي ثنائية كقولنا: هذا سيبويه و لذلك قالو ان كلاً منهما في محل اسم مرفوع تنبيها على اضمار الرابطة في النفس.
He said: And the proposition in the first case is called tripartite and in the second bipartite. The first because it contains in utterance three parts: the subject, the predicate, and the copula. The second because it contains two parts: the subject and the predicate. This is the well-known view among the majority. And in it [it is noted] that these two forms—i.e., the form where the copula is mentioned and the form where it is omitted—are either with mention of both terms or with omission of one. The reason for the naming in the first is apparent, but in the second it is not. For the proposition if it is with mention of the copula then it contains two parts, or with its omission then one part. Unless it is said that a partial appropriateness suffices in the naming, or that the prevalent case is mention of both terms, so the thing is named after the prevalent individuals. Then it is not hidden that the division into tripartite and bipartite as mentioned is only according to those who make 'huwa', 'hiya', and their like as copulas. But as for those who say that the copula is the rafa' vowel from the grammatical inflections and what runs in their course, he says: if the construction is from inflected words (mu'rabat), then the proposition is tripartite, like our saying 'Zaydun qa'imun'; and if it is from uninflected words (mabniyat), then it is bipartite, like our saying 'hadha Sibawayhi'. And therefore they said that each of them is in the place of a marfu' noun, as an alert to the implicit copula in the mind.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage expands on the definition of propositions as tripartite (thalithiyya) or bipartite (thuna'iyya). The standard view is that a proposition like 'Zaydun qa'imun' has three parts when the copula is explicit (e.g., 'Zaydun huwa qa'imun'), but two parts when the copula is omitted. The commentator notes a subtlety: if the copula is omitted, the proposition might be considered as having only one part (the subject and predicate merged?). However, the naming 'bipartite' is justified because the terms (subject and predicate) are still two, even if the copula is absent.

The passage then introduces an alternative view: those who consider the copula to be the rafa' (nominative) vowel rather than a separate word. On this view, for inflected words (mu'rabat), the proposition is tripartite because the rafa' vowel is an additional element; for uninflected words (mabniyat), the proposition is bipartite because there is no vowel change. In either case, the underlying logical structure includes an implicit copula in the mind.

This lesson is an expansion probe, explaining the reasoning behind the classification and addressing potential inconsistencies.

Key Terms
قضية ثلاثية
قضية ثنائية
رابطة

A) Tripartite, because the rafa' vowel on 'Zayd' and 'qa'im' is an extra part.
B) Bipartite, because the rafa' vowel is not considered a separate part.
C) Tripartite only if the copula 'huwa' is mentioned.
D) It depends on whether 'Zayd' is definite.

Key Takeaway
The classification of a proposition as tripartite or bipartite is not absolute; it depends on whether the copula is identified as a separate word or as a grammatical inflection.

Temporal and Non-Temporal Copulas in Arabic Logic
Author distinguishes temporal and non-temporal copulas with examples from Qur'an and mathematics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between temporal and non-temporal copulas in Arabic logical propositions and identify their functions.

Core Idea
The copula in Arabic logic wears two faces: one that tells time (kaana) and one that simply joins (huwa).

In this lesson, we explore how the Arabic language handles the logical copula—the link between subject and predicate. The passage from Sharh al-Matali' and the subsequent commentary clarify that while defective verbs like 'kaana' primarily indicate the time of attribution, they can also be used for timeless truths. For purely logical, non-temporal connections, Arabic employs pronouns like 'huwa'. Understanding this distinction is crucial when analyzing propositions in Islamic logic, as it affects how we interpret the modality and temporal scope of statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of copula
Find examples of 'kaana' and 'huwa' in the passage. Note that 'kaana' and its sisters are defective verbs that indicate time, while 'huwa' and its variants are non-temporal pronouns.
2
Understand the temporal function
The passage says defective verbs indicate 'zaman al-intisab' (time of the attribution) – present, past, or future. Their meaning is like a particle.
3
Recognize the non-temporal use of 'kaana'
The passage shows 'kaana' can be used for timeless truths, as in the Qur'anic verse 'wa kana Allahu ghafuran raheeman' where it does not indicate past time but an eternal attribute.
4
Observe the borrowing for non-temporal copula
For purely logical linking without time, Arabic borrows pronouns like 'huwa', 'hiya', and their dual and plural forms.
5
Apply the distinction
When reading a logical proposition in Arabic, identify whether the copula is temporal (using kaana) or non-temporal (using huwa) to determine if time is part of the judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في شرح المطالع و قد غلبت فى لغة العرب حتى انهم يستعملونها فيا ليس بزمانى كقوله تعالى و كان الله غفوراً رحيماً وفيا لا يختص بزمان كقولهم: «كل ثلاثة يكون فرداً». قوله هى الافعال المناقصة»: فانه انما يؤتى بها للاشعار بزمن الانتساب هل هو فى الحال او المضى او الاستقبال ؟ فمعناها حرف. قوله فاستعاروا للرابطة الغير الزمانية لفظة هو وهى ونحوهما من مواد التثنية والجمع المذكر والمؤنث.
He said in Sharh al-Matali': And it has become prevalent in the Arabic language to the point that they use it for what is not temporal, like His statement 'And Allah is (kana) Oft-Forgiving, Most Merciful' and for what is not restricted to time, like their saying 'Every three is (yakunu) odd.' He said: '[The defective verbs] are only brought to indicate the time of attribution, whether it is present, past, or future; so their meaning is a particle.' He said: 'So they borrowed for the non-temporal copula the word 'huwa' and 'hiya' and their likes from the forms of dual and plural, masculine and feminine.'
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage combines three related notes on the copula. The first, from Sharh al-Matali', explains that the verb 'kaana' can be used for non-temporal statements, as in the Qur'anic verse 'wa kana Allahu ghafuran raheeman' where 'kana' does not indicate past time but rather an eternal attribute, and in mathematical statements like 'kullu thalathatin yakunu fardan' (every three is odd) where 'yakunu' indicates a timeless truth.

The second note clarifies that defective verbs (al-af'al al-naqisa, i.e., kaana and its sisters) are used primarily to indicate the time of the attribution (zaman al-intisab) — whether present, past, or future. Their meaning is thus akin to a particle (harf) rather than a full verb.

The third note states that for the non-temporal copula (i.e., when no time is indicated), the Arabs borrowed words like 'huwa' (he) and 'hiya' (she) and their dual and plural forms, both masculine and feminine, to serve as a purely linking element between subject and predicate.

This lesson shows the subtlety of the copula in Arabic: temporal copulas (kaana) are defective verbs, while non-temporal copulas are pronouns used metaphorically.

Key Terms
كان وأخواتها
رابطة غير زمانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) To replace the pronoun 'huwa'.

Key Takeaway
The copula in Arabic logic can be temporal (kaana) or non-temporal (huwa), and understanding this distinction is essential for correctly parsing the time and modality of a proposition.

The Copula: A Particle, Not an Independent Noun
Author defends the non-independence of the copula.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to defend the claim that the copula in a proposition lacks independent meaning, using the transfer (naql) argument from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
The copula is a tool, not a thing.

This passage tackles a subtle but crucial point: the copula (rabita) in propositions like 'Zayd is a poet' might seem to be an independent noun (the word 'huwa'), but logicians argue it functions as a particle indicating the judgmental relation. We will explore the reasoning and the response to a key objection from grammar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about the copula's independence
The author states that the copula has no independent meaning; it is merely a state (hāla) between subject and predicate, a tool to know their condition. Therefore, the word indicating it is a particle (ḥarf) and a tool (ada), not a noun (ism).
The distinction between noun and particle is based on independence of meaning, as established earlier in the discussion of words.
2
Understand the objection from grammar
An objector might say: Grammarians explicitly state that 'huwa' in 'Zayd huwa shāʿir' refers back to Zayd, indicating him, which is an independent meaning suitable for predication. So how can it be a mere copula?
This objection relies on the original grammatical function of 'huwa' as a pronoun.
3
Follow the author's response: transfer (naql)
The author responds that originally 'huwa' is a noun, but rational scholars (arbāb al-maʿqūl) transferred it to express the judgmental relation (al-nisba al-ḥukmiyya). In its transferred usage, it only indicates that relation, not the subject Zayd.
The base text author (al-muṣannif) said: 'And huwa has been borrowed for it.' This borrowing refutes the objection.
4
Apply to defective verbs
The same reasoning applies to defective verbs (kāna, ṣāra, etc.). They appear to be independent verbs, but in their copulative function they are not independent; they were transferred from their original meanings.
The author notes that their independence is only according to the original coinage (aṣl al-waḍʿ).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حيث انه حالة بينهما وآلة لتعرف حالهما فلا يكون معنى مستقلاً يصلح لان يكون محكوماً عليه و به، فيكون اللفظ الدال عليها حرفاً و اداة إذ لاشك ان الاداتية والاسمية من جهة الاستقلال وعدمه كما سبق في باحث الألفاظ. :لا يقال : انهم قد صرحوا بان لفظة «هو» مثلاً فى قولنا : «زيد هو شاعر» يرجع الى زيد فيكون دالاً عليه وهو معنى مستقل قطعاً يصلح للاسناد اليه و به فكيف يكون رابطة ؟ لانا نقول: هذا بحسب الاصل و هو بهذه الحيثية اسم، لكن ارباب المعقول لما ارادوا ان يعبروا عن النسبة بلفظ نقلوا لفظة «هو» و «هی» و نحوهما الى النسبة فهى بهذه الحيثية لا تدل الاعلى النسبة الحكمية لا على «زيد» مثلاً ولذا قال المصنف: «و قد استعير لها هو» و بهذا يندفع ما يقال أيضاً من ان الافعال الناقصة مستقلات تدل على معان مستقلة ولو بطريق التضمن ولذا سميت افعالاً و كلمات فان ذلك ايضاً بحسب اصل الوضع (میرزا محمد علی)
Because it is a state between them and an instrument to know their condition, so it does not have an independent meaning suitable to be judged upon or by it. Thus the word indicating it is a particle and a tool, since there is no doubt that being a particle or a noun is with respect to independence and its lack, as previously in the investigation of words. It cannot be said: They have explicitly stated that the word 'huwa' for example in our saying 'Zayd huwa shaʿir' refers back to Zayd, thus it indicates him, and that is an independent meaning certainly suitable for attribution to and by it, so how can it be a copula? Because we say: This is with respect to the original, and in this respect it is a noun. But the rational scholars, when they wanted to express the relation with a word, transferred the word 'huwa' and 'hiya' and their likes to the relation, so in this respect they do not indicate anything except the judgmental relation, not 'Zayd' for example. Hence the author said: 'And 'huwa' has been borrowed for it.' And by this is refuted what is also said that the defective verbs are independent, indicating independent meanings even if by way of inclusion, and thus they are called verbs and words, for that too is according to the original coinage.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage addresses the nature of the copula in Arabic logic. The author begins by stating that the copula (rabita) is not an independent meaning; it is merely a relational state between the subject and predicate, serving as a tool to know their relationship. Therefore, the word that denotes it is a particle (harf) and a tool (ada), since the distinction between noun and particle is based on whether the meaning is independent or not. The author then anticipates an objection: Grammarians say that 'huwa' in 'Zayd huwa shāʿir' (Zayd is a poet) refers back to Zayd, indicating him, which is an independent meaning suitable for predication. The response is that originally 'huwa' is a noun, but logicians have transferred (naql) it to express the relational aspect, so in that usage it only indicates the judgmental relation, not the subject. The author cites the base text: 'And 'huwa' has been borrowed for it.' This also refutes the claim that defective verbs (al-afʿāl al-nāqiṣa) are independent because they seem to have independent meanings. The author explains that their independence is only in their original coinage, not in their logical function as copulae.

Key Terms
الرابطة
النسبة الحكمية

D) Because logicians do not use pronouns.

The copula in a proposition is a particle indicating the judgmental relation, not an independent noun.

Tripartite and Bipartite Propositions: Refining the Classification
Author explains and critiques the division of propositions into tripartite and bipartite based on mention of the copula.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a proposition is classified as tripartite or bipartite and evaluate the validity of this classification in light of different views on the copula.

Core Idea
The tripartite/bipartite division depends on both copula mention and completeness of the propositional parts.

The classification of propositions into tripartite (explicit copula) and bipartite (omitted copula) seems straightforward, but a deeper analysis reveals complications. This passage from a classical logic commentary critiques the standard rule by showing that the naming relies on specific assumptions about what counts as a copula and whether both subject and predicate are present. By carefully examining these conditions, you will sharpen your ability to evaluate categorical propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the majority view
The majority (al-jumhūr) define a tripartite proposition as one that verbally includes three parts: subject (maḥkūm ʿalayh), predicate (maḥkūm bih), and copula (rābiṭa). A bipartite proposition lacks the copula and has only subject and predicate.
2
Spot the objection
The author objects that the naming is problematic when one of the extremes (subject or predicate) is omitted. For example, if only the subject is mentioned and the copula is present, the proposition has only two parts (subject and copula), not three. Thus, the classification only holds when both extremes are mentioned.
3
Examine the defense
Two defenses are offered: (1) naming based on common usage (aghlab), i.e., the majority of cases have both extremes; (2) naming based on appropriateness (munāsaba) in some instances.
4
Distinguish two views of the copula
Some logicians take words like 'huwa' as copulae; for them, the tripartite/bipartite division is as stated. Others consider the copula to be the vowel of rafʿ (e.g., dammah) for declinable words. For the latter, a proposition like 'Zayd qā'im' is tripartite because the rafʿ vowel is a separate part, while 'hādhā Sībawayh' (indeclinable) is bipartite.
This shows that the classification depends on the grammatical nature of the terms.
5
Assess the refinement
The passage demonstrates that the simple tripartite/bipartite division is conditional: it requires both extremes to be mentioned and is sensitive to the view of the copula. You should now be able to state these conditions precisely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله والقضية على الاول تسمى ثلاثية وعلى الثاني ثنائية اما الاول فلاشتمالها في اللفظ على ثلاثة اجزاء المحكوم عليه والمحكوم به والرابطة . و اما الثاني فلاشتمالها على جزئين منها : المحكوم عليه والمحكوم به هذا هو المشهور عند الجمهور. و فيه ان هاتين الصورتين اعنى صورة ان تذكر الرابطة وصورة ان تحذف اما ان تكونا مع ذكر الطرفين أو مع حذف احدهما و وجه التسمية على الاول ظاهر واما على الثاني فلا، فان القضية ح كانت مع ذكر الرابطة فتشتمل على جزئين او مع حذفها فعلى جزء واحد. اللهم الا ان يقال: أنه يكفى في التسمية وجود المناسبة فى البعض أو يقال ان الاغلب هو ذكر الطرفين فسمى الشيء باسم اغلب الأفراد. ثم لا يخفى ان التقسيم الثلاثية والثنائية على ما ذكر، انما هو عند من يجعل «هو» و «هي» و نحوهما روابط و اما عند من يقول : ان الرابطة حركة الرفع من الحركات الاعرابية وما يجرى مجربها فهو يقول: ان التركيب ان كان من المعربات فالقضية ثلاثية كقولنا : «زيد قائم» و ان كان من المبنيات فهي ثنائية كقولنا: هذا سيبويه و لذلك قالو ان كلاً منهما في محل اسم مرفوع تنبيها على اضمار الرابطة في النفس. (میرزا محمد علی)
His statement: And the proposition in the first case is called tripartite and in the second bipartite. As for the first, because it verbally includes three parts: the judged-upon, the judged-by, and the copula. As for the second, because it includes two of them: the judged-upon and the judged-by. This is the well-known view among the majority. And there is [an objection] in it: that these two forms—namely the form where the copula is mentioned and the form where it is omitted—can be either with the mention of both extremes or with the omission of one of them. The reason for the naming is clear in the first [when both extremes are mentioned] but not in the second [when one is omitted], because then if the proposition is with the mention of the copula, it would include two parts, or if without it, then [only] one part. Unless it is said that it suffices for the naming that there is appropriateness in some cases, or that the majority is the mention of both extremes, so the thing is named by the name of the majority of individuals. Then it is not hidden that the division into tripartite and bipartite, as mentioned, is only for those who make 'huwa' and 'hiya' and their likes copulae. As for those who say that the copula is the vowel of rafʿ from the declensional vowels and what runs in their course, they say: if the compound is from declinable words, then the proposition is tripartite like our saying 'Zayd qā'im', and if from indeclinable words, then it is bipartite like our saying 'hādhā Sībawayh'. Therefore they said that each of them is in the place of a nominative noun, indicating the implicitation of the copula in the soul.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the classification of propositions into tripartite (thalāthiyya) and bipartite (thunāʾiyya). The author first states the majority view: a proposition is tripartite if it verbally includes subject (maḥkūm ʿalayh), predicate (maḥkūm bih), and copula (rābiṭa); it is bipartite if it includes only subject and predicate. However, the author objects that this naming is problematic when one of the extremes (subject or predicate) is omitted. For instance, if only the subject is mentioned but the copula is also mentioned, it would actually have two parts (subject and copula) not three. The objection is that the naming only works when both extremes are mentioned. The defense is that the naming is based on the most common cases (aghlab) or appropriateness (munāsaba). Furthermore, the division depends on the view of the copula. For those who consider words like 'huwa' as copulae, the division is as stated. For those who consider the copula to be the vowel of rafʿ (like the dammah in a declinable word), then if the terms are declinable (muʿrab), the proposition is tripartite because the vowel itself is a separate part (e.g., 'Zayd qā'im' with the rafʿ on both); if indeclinable (mabnī), it is bipartite (e.g., 'hādhā Sībawayh'). This shows the nuanced view of different scholars.

Key Terms
القضية الثلاثية
القضية الثنائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The proposition would then have only one or two parts, not three.
C) The majority of logicians reject the term 'tripartite.'
D) It only applies to indeclinable words.

Key Takeaway
The tripartite/bipartite classification is not absolute; it depends on whether both extremes are mentioned and on the view of the copula (lexical vs. grammatical).

The Non-temporal Use of 'Kāna' in Arabic Logic and Language
Author explains that 'kana' is used in Arabic for non-temporal relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify non-temporal uses of 'kāna' in Arabic logical and Qur'anic contexts and explain why it does not always indicate past tense.

Core Idea
Kāna in logic is a copula, not always a past tense verb.

In Arabic grammar, 'kāna' is primarily a past tense verb. However, in logic and rhetoric, it often functions as a copula linking subject and predicate without temporal restrictions. This passage from Sharḥ al-Muṭāliʿ highlights two classic examples: the Qur'anic verse 'wa kāna Allāhu ghafūran raḥīman' and the mathematical statement 'kullu thalāthatin yakūnu fardan'. Understanding this non-temporal use is essential for correctly interpreting propositions in logic and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two examples provided
The passage gives two distinct examples: one from the Qur'an (wa kāna Allāhu ghafūran raḥīman) and one from everyday language (kullu thalāthatin yakūnu fardan). Locate them in the Arabic text.
Note that the second example uses the imperfect form yakūnu, but it serves the same copulative function.
2
Analyze the temporal status of each example
In the Qur'anic example, 'kāna' describes an eternal attribute of Allah — it is not limited to the past. In the mathematical example, 'yakūnu' expresses a timeless logical relation: being odd is inherent to every three, regardless of time.
Consider what would change if 'kāna' were interpreted as strictly past tense in these contexts.
3
Understand the larger logical significance
In formal logic (mantiq), propositions are often formed using 'kāna' or its derivatives as a copula. Recognizing that 'kāna' can be temporally neutral allows logicians to analyze statements about eternal truths (e.g., theological propositions) without temporal connotations.
This flexibility is crucial for the analysis of categorical propositions in Islamic logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في شرح المطالع و قد غلبت فى لغة العرب حتى انهم يستعملونها فيا ليس بزمانى كقوله تعالى و كان الله غفوراً رحيماً وفيا لا يختص بزمان كقولهم: «كل ثلاثة يكون فرداً». (میرزا محمد علی)
He said in Sharḥ al-Muṭāliʿ: And it has become prevalent in the language of the Arabs that they use it in what is not temporal, like His saying, exalted: 'And Allah is Forgiving, Merciful' (Qur'an 4:106), and in what is not specific to a time, like their saying: 'Every three is odd.'

Two examples are given: one from the Qur'an: 'wa kāna Allāhu ghafūran raḥīman' (And Allah is Forgiving, Merciful), where 'kāna' indicates a permanent attribute, not a past tense. The other is a mathematical statement: 'kullu thalāthatin yakūnu fardan' (Every three is odd), where 'yakūnu' indicates a universal relation, not bound by time. This shows that 'kāna' can function as a copula without temporal implications.

Defective Verbs as Temporal Copulae
Defective verbs indicate time of attribution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify defective verbs (al-afʿāl al-munāqiṣa) in logical propositions and explain their role as temporal copulae.

Core Idea
Defective verbs are temporal markers, not independent predicates.

In Arabic logic, the copula (nisba) links subject and predicate, often expressed by defective verbs like kāna. This passage clarifies that such verbs only indicate tense (present, past, future) and carry no independent meaning, functioning like particles. Understanding this helps parse propositions correctly in Islamic logic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defective verb
Look for verbs like kāna, ṣāra, laysa, and their sisters. In logical propositions, these verbs connect subject and predicate.
In Arabic, these are called al-afʿāl al-munāqiṣa.
2
Determine the tense indicated
The verb shows whether the attribution (nisba) is in the present (ḥāl), past (maḍī), or future (istiqbāl).
For example, kāna indicates past, yakūnu indicates present or future.
3
Check if the verb adds extra meaning
Defective verbs do not assert existence or action; they only mark time. If the verb can be replaced by a temporal particle, it is defective.
This distinguishes them from perfect verbs that carry full meaning.
4
Confirm with the 'particle' test
The passage says their meaning is that of a particle (harf). So ask: does the verb merely indicate tense without adding a new event? If yes, it is a defective verb.
Particles in Arabic are function words like fī or with.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله هى الافعال المناقصة»: فانه انما يؤتى بها للاشعار بزمن الانتساب هل هو فى الحال او المضى او الاستقبال ؟ فمعناها حرف (التقريب ص ٤١)
His statement: 'They are the defective verbs': For they are only brought to indicate the time of the attribution: whether it is in the present, the past, or the future? So their meaning is a particle (harf). (al-Taqrīb, p. 41)
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This is a short gloss on the phrase 'al-afʿāl al-munāqiṣa' (the defective verbs), which include verbs like 'kāna' and its sisters. The author explains that these verbs are used solely to indicate the temporal dimension of the attribution (zaman al-intisāb): whether it is present (ḥāl), past (maḍī), or future (istiqbāl). Therefore, their meaning is like that of a particle (harf), not an independent noun or verb. This reinforces the idea that copulae are not independent meanings.

Key Terms
