The Grammatical Status of Ḍamīr al-Faṣl: Noun or Particle?
Critique of the claim that 'huwa' must be a noun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the dispute over whether 'huwa' is a noun or a particle and evaluate the arguments from both grammarians and logicians.

Core Idea
The classification of 'huwa' as noun or particle depends on disciplinary perspective, not consensus.

This passage examines a long-standing dispute between grammarians and logicians over the word 'huwa' when used as a separating pronoun (ḍamīr al-faṣl). The author critiques the claim that it must be a noun because it refers back to the subject. He shows that even if grammarians agreed it is a noun, logicians are free to call it a tool for their own purposes. Understanding this dispute sharpens your awareness of how technical terms shift meaning across disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting positions
One side says 'huwa' is a noun (because it is a pronoun referring to the subject). The other says it is a particle used as a copula. Locate these positions in the passage.
The original author (Muḥaqqiq) takes the first view; the commentator critiques it.
2
Analyze the first argument (grammarians' consensus)
The objection claims grammarians agree 'huwa' is a noun. The author counters by citing Ibn Hishām and al-Raḍī who say it is a particle according to most Baṣrans. Check the passage for these references.
Ibn Hishām's al-Mughnī is a major source of Arabic grammar.
3
Evaluate the second argument (logicians' independence)
Even if grammarians agreed, logicians are not bound to follow them; they can define it as a tool for logical analysis. Notice how the author asserts this independence.
This illustrates the autonomy of logical terminology.
4
Distinguish between the medial and final 'huwa'
The disputed 'huwa' is the separating pronoun (ḍamīr al-faṣl) between subject and predicate. The final hidden 'huwa' in a verb like 'yaktubu' is the subject (fāʿil) and is unanimously a noun. This resolves the imagined repetition.
Understanding this distinction prevents confusion about redundancy.
5
Assess the author's conclusion
The author shows that al-Muḥaqqiq's attempt to justify the logicians' usage is unsatisfactory to them. The passage ends with a clarification of which pronoun is in dispute.
The commentator's critique is itself a refinement of the original position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله مع كونهما في الأصل اسماء لا ادوات لان الضمير راجع الى الموضوع فيكون معناه متحداً معه ذاتاً. وايضاً صرح النحاة بان كلمة «هو» و تحوها اسماء فيجب ان يكون معانيها مستقلة فلا يصح جعلها من الاداة. : وفيهما نظر اما فى الاول فلانه انما يصح اذا سلم كونه اسماً و اما اذا قلنا: إنه حرف اتي للرابطة و ان كان فى صورة الاسم فلا وقد صرح الشيخ بكونه أداة على ما نقله بعض المحشين. و اما في الثاني: فلانه ليس متفقاً عليه فيها و قد صرح ابن هشام في المغني بان ضمير الفصل حرف عنداكثر البصريين و الى هذا ذهب الرضى (ره) على انه لو فرضنا اجماع النحاة على انه اسم فلا يلزم ان يكون اداة عندنا فان القوم يصرحون يكونه اداة فظهر ظهور النور على الطور ان ما ذكره المصنف فى توجيه كلام القوم و هو تسميتهم الضمير بالاداة توجيه بما لم يرضوابه . و اعلم ان هذا الضمير الذى اختلف في كونه اداة او اسماً هو الضمير الذي يسميه النحاة فصلاً و عماداً و اما غيره فلا خلاف في كونه اسماً ومن هذا يندفع ما يتوهم من انا اذا اقلنا : «زيد يكتب» لكان لفظة هو مقدرة فى آخر الكلمة مستكنة فيها فلو ذكرنا الرابطة ايضاً لكنا قلنا: زيد يكتب هو وانه تكرار. و وجه الدفع: ان التكرار انما يلزم لوكان احدهما عين الآخر وهو ممنوع فان لفظة «هو» التي في آخر الكلمة ليست برابطة عندهم بل هى فاعل والمتوسطة رابطة واحديهما غير الأخرى ولذا اتفقوا على كون المتأخرة اسماً واختلفوا في المتقدمة. (شيخ عبد الرحيم)
His statement: 'While they are originally nouns, not particles, because the pronoun refers back to the subject, so its meaning is identical with it in essence. Also, the grammarians have explicitly stated that the word 'huwa' and its likes are nouns, so their meanings must be independent, and thus it is not valid to make them particles.' And there is consideration in both: As for the first, it is only valid if it is conceded that it is a noun. But if we say that it is a particle that comes for the copula, even if it is in the form of a noun, then [the argument fails]. And the Shaykh (Ibn Sīnā?) has explicitly stated that it is a tool, as reported by some commentators. As for the second, it is not agreed upon concerning it. Ibn Hishām has explicitly stated in al-Mughnī that the pronoun of separation (ḍamīr al-faṣl) is a particle according to most Baṣrans, and al-Raḍī (may God have mercy on him) went to this. Moreover, even if we assume that the grammarians are unanimous that it is a noun, it does not necessarily follow that it is a tool for us [logicians], for the [logicians] explicitly state that it is a tool. So it has become clear as clear as daylight that what the author mentioned as a justification of the [logicians'] statement—that they call the pronoun a tool—is a justification that they would not be satisfied with. And know that this pronoun, concerning which there is disagreement as to whether it is a tool or a noun, is the pronoun that the grammarians call 'separator' (faṣl) and 'support' (ʿimād). As for other [pronouns], there is no disagreement that it is a noun. From this is refuted what is imagined: that if we say 'Zayd yaktubu' (Zayd writes), the word 'huwa' is supposed at the end of the word, hidden in it, so if we mention the copula as well, we would say 'Zayd yaktubu huwa' and that would be repetition. The rebuttal: repetition would only be necessary if one were exactly the same as the other, which is denied. For the word 'huwa' at the end of the word is not a copula according to them; rather, it is a subject (fāʿil), and the medial [huwa] is a copula, and one is not the other. Therefore they agreed that the latter is a noun and disputed about the former. (Shaykh ʿAbd al-Raḥīm)
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage addresses the grammatical status of the pronoun 'huwa' when used as a copula. The objection is raised: since 'huwa' is originally a pronoun referring to the subject, it must be a noun, so it cannot be a particle (adāh). The author scrutinizes this objection with two points. First, if we accept that 'huwa' is a noun, the argument holds; but if we claim it is a particle used for the copula (even if in the form of a noun), then it is not a noun. The author references a 'Shaykh' (likely Avicenna) who explicitly called it a tool. Second, the claim that grammarians agree it is a noun is false; Ibn Hishām in al-Mughnī states that the pronoun of separation (ḍamīr al-faṣl) is a particle according to most Basran grammarians, and al-Raḍī (al-Astarābādī) agreed. Even if there were consensus among grammarians, logicians (ahl al-maʿqūl) are under no obligation to follow them; they can define it as a tool. The author then clarifies that the disputed pronoun is specifically the 'faṣl' or 'ʿimād' (the separating pronoun that comes between subject and predicate), not other pronouns. This resolves a potential confusion: if one says 'Zayd yaktubu' (Zayd writes), the pronoun 'huwa' is implicitly the subject (fāʿil) of the verb, not a copula. If we add a copula, it would be 'Zayd yaktubu huwa' which is not redundant because the first 'huwa' (hidden) is the subject and the second (medial) is the copula. Hence, no repetition. The grammarians agree that the latter (hidden subject pronoun) is a noun, but they dispute the former (medial copula).

Key Terms
ضمير الفصل (ḍamīr al-faṣl): The separating pronoun that comes between subject and predicate, e.g., 'huwa' in 'Zayd huwa qāʾim'. [Note: In grammar, some call it a particle; in logic, it functions as a copula.]
العماد (al-ʿimād): The support pronoun, same as ḍamīr al-faṣl, used to emphasize the predicate. [Note: Synonym for ḍamīr al-faṣl in Arabic grammar.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who among the grammarians considered 'ḍamīr al-faṣl' a particle?
A) Sibawayh
B) Ibn Hishām
C) Al-Zamakhsharī
D) None

Key Takeaway
The classification of 'huwa' as a noun or particle is not settled; it depends on whether one adopts a grammatical or logical perspective.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Understanding the Term 'Shartiyya' in Islamic Logic
Author discusses the meaning of 'shartiyya' as the whole conditional or just the protasis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the primary and secondary referents of the term 'shartiyya' in classical Islamic logical texts.

Core Idea
'Shartiyya' usually means the whole conditional, not just the 'if' clause.

In Islamic logic, the term 'shartiyya' (conditional proposition) is used in multiple ways. This passage from a logical commentary clarifies its primary meaning and occasional shifts. Understanding this terminology is crucial for correctly interpreting logical arguments that involve conditionals, such as those used in Kalam proofs or Usul al-Fiqh reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary meaning of 'shartiyya'
The passage states that 'shartiyya' most commonly refers to the entire conditional statement, i.e., the combination of the condition (sharṭ) and the consequent (jazāʾ).
2
Recognise the secondary usage
Sometimes the consequent alone is called 'shartiyya' because of its connection to the condition. This is a lenient usage.
3
Note the rare usage for the condition alone
Very rarely does 'shartiyya' denote only the condition. This is exceptional.
4
Understand the poetic illustration
The author gives a corrupt poetic line where 'al-lahdam' is used to refer to 'al-lahṭamī' as a metaphor. This illustrates how a term can shift in meaning by association, similar to 'shartiyya' shifting from the whole to the part.
The poetic example is textually uncertain, but the point about metaphorical naming is clear.

This passage discusses the terminology of conditional propositions (sharṭiyya). Conditional and Disjunctive Propositions Defined
Author defines conditional and disjunctive propositions with positive and negative forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish conditional (muttasila) from disjunctive (munfasila) propositions, and affirmative from negative forms, using examples from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Conditional and disjunctive propositions state relations between propositions, not their truth values.

This lesson unpacks the definitions of conditional and disjunctive propositions as presented in the classical text. You will learn to recognize their structure, understand that they do not assert truth of their parts, and identify the affirmative/negative variations. Mastering these distinctions is essential for analyzing more complex arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of proposition
Read the proposition and determine if it connects two propositions with a conditional particle (like 'if' or 'whenever') – that is a conditional (muttaṣila). If it uses a disjunctive particle (like 'either...or') indicating opposition, it is a disjunctive (munfaṣila).
The particle 'law' or 'in' often indicates a conditional; 'immā' signals a disjunctive.
2
Check for affirmation or negation
See if the proposition asserts the relation (affirmative) or denies it (negative). For conditionals, affirmative: 'If A then B'; negative: 'It is not the case that if A then B'. For disjunctives, affirmative: 'Either A or B'; negative: 'It is not the case that either A or B'.
Negation is often signaled by 'laysa al-batta' or 'mā' etc.
3
Analyze the relations within
For conditionals, note the antecedent and consequent and the dependence between them. For disjunctives, note the two relations and their opposition (munāfāt). The text emphasizes that the truth of the parts is not asserted.
The eight forms arise from combining affirmative/negative antecedent and consequent (in conditionals) or affirmative/negative of each relation (in disjunctives).
4
Extract examples from the text
The author gives examples: conditional affirmative – 'If the sun is rising, the day exists'; conditional negative – 'It is not the case that whenever the sun is rising, the night exists'. Disjunctive affirmative – 'Either this number is even or odd'; disjunctive negative – 'It is not the case that either this number is odd or three'. Practice identifying the type and polarity.
The author challenges you to derive the remaining six examples yourself.

(14) His saying: 'by affirming a relation upon another condition': such as 'If the sun is rising, then the day exists', and the judgment of the existence of the day is dependent on the judgment of the rising of the sun. So the conditional proposition by itself, i.e., without external indication, indicates neither truth nor falsehood, because it does not state the judgment of the predicate for the subject in the antecedent so that the consequent could be proven by analogy with the antecedent. For this reason it is said: conditional propositions do not entail truth. (15) His saying: 'or negating that affirmation': i.e., negating the dependence of the consequent on the antecedent, such as 'It is not the case that whenever the sun is rising, the night exists.' And the first is called affirmative and the second negative, whether both relations are affirmative, both negative, or different; thus there are eight forms. And all that applies to his saying: 'or by opposition', so you must extract the examples we have left. (16) His saying: 'or by opposition between the two relations': i.e., or the judgment is of opposition between the two relations that are entered by the disjunctive particle, such as 'Either this number is even or it is odd.' So the relation of evenness to the indicated number opposes the relation of oddness to it, and vice versa. (17) His saying: 'or by negating that opposition': such as 'It is not the case that either this number is odd or it is three.' For the relations of oddness and threeness to it are not incompatible.

Commentary
This passage defines the conditional (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila) propositions, both affirmative and negative. It begins with the conditional: a proposition that states a relation between two propositions on a condition (taqdir), like 'If the sun is rising, the day exists.' The author clarifies that such a proposition does not assert the truth of the antecedent or consequent; it only states the dependence. Then he gives the negative conditional: e.g., 'It is not the case that whenever the sun is rising, the night exists.' He notes that there are eight possible forms based on the combinations of affirmative/negative antecedent and consequent relations. Next, he defines the disjunctive: a proposition that states opposition (munafaat) between two relations, like 'Either this number is even or odd.' The negative disjunctive denies that opposition, e.g., 'It is not the case that either this number is odd or three.' The author emphasizes that the same eight-form structure applies to disjunctives as well.

Conditional and disjunctive propositions express relations (dependence or opposition) between propositions, not the truth of the parts themselves.

Why Negative Propositions Retain Their Names
Author explains why negative propositions retain the names categorical/conditional/disjunctive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the rationale for naming negative categorical, conditional, and disjunctive propositions using the concepts of transfer (naql) and potential (isti'dad).

Core Idea
Negatives borrow names from affirmatives by transfer or potential, not by actual content.

In logic, we often classify propositions as categorical, conditional, or disjunctive—both in affirmative and negative forms. But why call a negative conditional 'conditional' when it denies any connection? This passage from Yazdi's Hashiya clarifies the basis: the names are derived from the affirmative prototype or from the potential to become affirmative. Understanding this prevents confusion when dealing with negative propositions in logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Notice that negative propositions (e.g., 'Zayd did not stand' or 'If not A then B') do not actually express predication, connection, or disjunction. Yet they are still called categorical, conditional, or disjunctive. Ask why this is so.
This tension drives the entire discussion.
2
Extract the three initial views
The text mentions views: (1) resemblance to affirmatives in structure, (2) being opposites of affirmatives, (3) the preferred view: transfer of names from linguistic to technical meaning because affirmatives suit the definition.
The third view is highlighted as 'الاولى' (more appropriate).
3
Understand the fourth view: potential (isti'dad)
Another justification: negative propositions have the potential to become affirmative if the negation particle is removed. For example, 'ما قام زيد' would be 'قام زيد' without negation, which is a proper attribution. This potential justifies the name.
The author supports this with reference to grammarians who call such negatives 'rational truths' (حقيقة عقلية).
4
Apply to disjunctives: the special case
The potential view does not apply directly to disjunctives because removing the disjunction particle does not yield a conditional. Instead, a disjunctive implies a conditional (e.g., 'either even or odd' is equivalent to 'if even then not odd, if odd then not even'). So the naming is justified by implication.
The text gives concrete arithmetic and natural examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۸) قوله «فاولالى شرطية متصلة»: اعلم ان تسمية القضاياء بالحملية والمتصلة والمنفصلة في الموجبات ظاهرة لتحقق معنى الحمل والاتصال والانفصال، واما في السوالب فقال بعضهم المشابهتها اياها في الاطراف او لكونها مقابلات لها والا فقد حكم فيها بسلب الحمل والاتصال والانفصال. والاولى ان يقال انهم نقلوا هذه الاسامى من معانيها اللغوية الى المفهومات الاصطلاحية لوجود المناسبة في بعض افرادها اعنى الموجبات وذلك القدر يكفي في صحة النقل. او نقول ان تسمية السوالب بهذه الاسامى لان لاجزائها استعداد قبول الحمل و الاتصال و الانفصال بمعنى انها لو سلبت منها اداة السلب لكانت حملية ومتصلة ومنفصلة. اترى ان ارباب المعانى يسمون نحو ما قام زيد و ما خرج من المنفيات، حقيقة عقلية مع انهم عرفوها بانها اسناد الفعل أو معناه الى ما هو له عند المتكلم فى الظاهر و لم يسند القيام الى زيد ولا الخروج الى بكر اصلاً فضلاً عن ان يكون الى ما هو له لمجرد ملاحظة الاستعداد والصلاحية، قال المصنف : وذلك، لانه لو اعتبر الكلام مجرداً عن النفى و ادى بصورة الاثبات لكان اسناداً إلى ما هو له لان النقي فرع الاثبات فالاسناد فى قام زيد الى ما هوله فيكون حقيقة و كذا اذا نفيته وقلت: ماقام زيد انتهى. و قد تبين مماتلونا عليك حال تسمية المنفصلة بالشرطية ايضاً لكن الوجه الاخير لا يجرى هنا فانها لا تصير المنفصلة بحذف اداة الانفصال متصلة اللهم الا ان يلاحظ فيها المتصلة اللازمة لها فان قولنا : «اما ان يكون هذا العدد زوجاً او فرداً في قوة قولنا: ان كان هذا العدد زوجاً لم يكن فرداً و ان كان فرداً لم يكن زوجاً» و كذا قولنا: (اما ان يكون هذا الشيء حجراً أو شجراً في قوة قولنا: ان كان هذا الشيء حجراً لم يكن شجراً و ان كان شجراً لم يكن حجراً وعلى هذا القياس. وقد تبين أيضاً من ذلك معنى قولهم ان الشرطية لا يوجد في شيء من طرفيها الحكم بل فرضه ، فان المنفصلة و ان لم يكن فى ظاهرها فرض الحكم ايضاً الا انها فى حكم المتصلة التي فيها فرض الحكم فتنته.
(18) His saying: 'Then the first is a conditional connected...' Know that naming propositions as categorical, conditional, and disjunctive in affirmatives is clear due to the realization of the meaning of predication, connection, and disconnection. As for negatives, some said [they are so named] due to their resemblance to affirmatives in terms of the parts, or because they are their opposites; otherwise, they would have been judged to negate predication, connection, and disconnection. The more appropriate [view] is to say that they transferred these names from their linguistic meanings to technical concepts because of the suitability found in some of their instances, namely the affirmatives, and that much is sufficient for the validity of the transfer. Or we may say that the naming of negatives with these names is because their parts have the potential to accept predication, connection, and disconnection, meaning that if the negation particle were removed from them, they would be categorical, conditional, and disjunctive. Do you not see that the masters of meaning call expressions like 'ما قام زيد' (Zaid did not stand) and 'ما خرج' (he did not go out) a rational truth, even though they define it as the attribution of an action or its meaning to that which it is for, according to the speaker, in appearance, and [in these examples] the standing was not attributed to Zaid nor the going out to Bakr at all, let alone being attributed to that which it is for, merely due to considering potential and suitability? The author said: 'That is because if the speech were considered stripped of negation and expressed in affirmative form, it would be an attribution to that which it is for, because the negative is derivative of the affirmative. Thus the attribution in "Zaid stood" is to that which it is for, so it is a truth. Similarly, if you negate it and say "Zaid did not stand" [it is also considered in terms of potential].' End quote. From what we have recited to you, the situation of naming the disjunctive as conditional also becomes clear. However, the last [third] approach does not apply here, because the disjunctive does not become a conditional by removing the disjunctive particle, unless one considers the conditional that is necessarily implied by it. For our saying: 'Either this number is even or odd' is equivalent to our saying: 'If this number is even, then it is not odd, and if it is odd, then it is not even.' Similarly, our saying: 'Either this thing is a stone or a tree' is equivalent to our saying: 'If this thing is a stone, then it is not a tree, and if it is a tree, then it is not a stone.' And so on. From this, the meaning of their statement 'The conditional has no judgment in either of its two parts, but rather a supposition' also becomes clear. For even though the disjunctive does not have in its apparent form a supposition of judgment, it is nevertheless in the position of the conditional that contains a supposition of judgment. So understand.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage discusses why negative propositions are still called categorical, conditional, or disjunctive. The author presents three views: (1) because they resemble affirmatives in their parts, (2) because they are opposites of affirmatives, and (3) the more appropriate view: the names were transferred from linguistic to technical meanings due to suitability in affirmatives. He also mentions a fourth view: negatives have potential readiness (isti'dad) to become affirmatives if the negation is removed. He supports this by citing grammarians who call negative sentences like 'ما قام زيد' rational truths because they have the potential for attribution. He then applies this to disjunctives: the third view (potential) does not apply because removing the disjunctive particle does not yield a conditional; rather, a disjunctive implies a conditional (e.g., 'either even or odd' is equivalent to 'if even then not odd, if odd then not even'). Thus, even negative disjunctives are named after affirmatives by implication. Finally, he clarifies that both conditionals and disjunctives are characterized by supposition (fard) of judgment rather than actual judgment.

Key Terms
تسمية (tasmiya)
حملية (hamliyya)
استعداد (isti'dad)
نقي (naqi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it resembles affirmatives in its parts.
B) Because the name was transferred from linguistic to technical meaning due to suitability in affirmatives.
C) Because removing the negation particle would turn it into a disjunctive.
D) Because it is the opposite of an affirmative disjunctive.

Key Takeaway
Negative propositions retain their names from affirmatives either by linguistic transfer or by potential, and disjunctives even imply conditionals, upholding the classification.

Explicitness in Propositional Definitions
Author addresses objection that definitions of muttasila and munfasila are interchangeable, and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objection that the definitions of conditional and disjunctive are interchangeable, and reconstruct the author's defense based on the principle of sarahah (explicitness).

Core Idea
Definitions in logic require explicit wording, not implied meaning.

In this passage, the author faces a clever objection: the definitions of conditional (muttsila) and disjunctive (munfasila) seem to apply to each other's examples. For instance, a disjunctive can be rephrased as conditionals, and a conditional can be understood as a disjunction. If so, the definitions become interchangeable and thus invalid. The author defends the definitions by insisting on explicitness (sarahah): the definitions require that the judgment be stated explicitly, not merely implied. This principle is crucial for working with propositional definitions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection (naqd)
The objector claims that the definitions of muttasila and munfasila are interchangeable. For example, the disjunctive 'Either this number is even or odd' can be seen as a conditional (if even then not odd, etc.), and the conditional 'If this number is even then it is not odd' can be seen as a disjunctive (since it implies opposition). This would make the definitions invalid.
The objector uses a concrete numerical example to illustrate the overlap.
2
Analyze the first response (denial of premise)
The author's first move is to deny that the conditional 'If this number is even, then it is not odd' is a simple conditional. He argues it is actually a disjunctive composed of two conditionals. This preserves the distinction between simple and compound propositions.
This response questions whether the example is truly a conditional.
3
Analyze the second response (sarahah principle)
Even if the example were conceded, the author argues that definitions require explicit (sarahah) judgment, not implied (istilzam). The disjunctive does not explicitly state a conditional relation; it only implies it. Similarly, the conditional does not explicitly state opposition. Therefore, the definitions remain distinct based on what is directly stated.
Explicitness is the key: a definition applies only when the judgment is made in plain words, not through implication.
4
Extend the principle to categorical propositions
The author extends the same reasoning to a potential objection against the definition of categorical (hamliyya). A conditional like 'If the sun is rising, then the day exists' implies a categorical judgment (the rising of the sun implies the existence of the day), but it does not state it explicitly. Hence the categorical definition is not contradicted.
This shows the broad applicability of the sarahah principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال: فعلى هذا يختل تعريف كل واحد من المتصلة والمنفصلة منعاً فانه يصدق على قولنا : «اما ان يكون هذا العدد زوجاً او فرداً مع انه منفصلة انه حكم فيه بثبوت نسبة على تقدير اخرى حيث انه في معنى قولنا ان كان هذا العدد زوجاً لم يكن فرداً و ان كان فرداً لم يكن زوجاً و ايضاً يصدق على انه متصلة انه قد حكم فيه بالمنافاة بين النسبتين فانه في قوة قولنا : «اما ان يكون هذا العدد زوجاً او فرداً». لا نقول: أنا لانسلم ان نحو قولنا: ان كان هذا العدد زوجاً لم يكن فرداً و ان كان فرداً لم يكن زوجاً متصلة بل نقول انه منفصلة مركبة من المتصلتين، ولو سلم فنقول: إن المراد في كل واحد من تعريف المتصلة والمنفصلة الحكم بالصراحة ولا حكم لناصريحاً بالثبوت على تقدير اخرى في قولنا : «أما ان يكون هذا العدد...» ولا بالمنافات بين النسبتين فى قولنا ان كان هذا العدد زوجاً لم يكن فرداً...» و ما ذكرته انما يأتي لوكان المراد مطلق الحكم بالثبوت و الحكم بالمنافاة وليس كذلك فان المطلق ينصرف الى اكمل الأفراد و اشهرها وبهذا يندفع ايضاً ما ربما يتوهم من ان تعريف الحملية منتقض بالقضاياء الشرطية، لأنه يصدق عليها انه حكم فيها بثبوت شيء لشيء أو نفيه عنه فان معنى قولنا مثلاً: ان كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود ان طلوع الشمس مستلزم لوجود النهار و كذا معنى قولنا : ليس ان كانت الشمس طالعة فالليل موجود ان طلوع الشمس ليس بمستلزم لوجود الليل وذلك لما ذكر من ان المراد من الحكم بالثبوت او السلب التصريحى لا الاستلزامي فتأمل.
It cannot be said: On this, the definition of each of the conditional and disjunctive becomes invalid, because it would apply to our saying: 'Either this number is even or odd' – although it is a disjunctive – that it is a judgment of affirming a relation upon another condition, since it is equivalent to our saying: 'If this number is even, then it is not odd, and if it is odd, then it is not even.' And also it would apply to it that it is a conditional, because it is a judgment of opposition between the two relations, since it is equivalent to our saying: 'Either this number is even or odd.' We say: We do not concede that our saying: 'If this number is even, then it is not odd, and if it is odd, then it is not even' is a conditional; rather we say it is a disjunctive composed of two conditionals. And even if it were conceded, we say: What is meant in each of the definitions of the conditional and disjunctive is explicit judgment, and there is no explicit judgment of affirming a relation upon another condition in our saying: 'Either this number...' nor of opposition between the two relations in our saying: 'If this number is even, then it is not odd...' And what you mentioned would only follow if what was meant was absolute judgment of affirmation and judgment of opposition, but it is not so; for the absolute is taken to refer to the most perfect and most well-known individuals. And by this is also repelled what might be imagined, that the definition of categorical is refuted by conditional propositions, because it would apply to them that they are judgments of affirming something of something or negating it from it. For the meaning of our saying, e.g., 'If the sun is rising, then the day exists' is that the rising of the sun implies the existence of the day, and similarly the meaning of our saying: 'It is not the case that if the sun is rising, then the night exists' is that the rising of the sun does not imply the existence of the night. And that is because, as mentioned, what is meant by the judgment of affirmation or negation is the explicit one, not the implied one. So reflect.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange (naqd wa jawab). The objector claims that the definitions of muttasila and munfasila are interchangeable: the disjunctive 'Either A or B' can be seen as affirming a relation on a condition (since it implies conditionals), and the conditional 'If A then not B' can be seen as a judgment of opposition (since it amounts to a disjunctive). The author gives two responses. First, he denies that the conditional 'If A then not B' is a simple conditional; rather it is a disjunctive composed of two conditionals. Second, he argues that the definitions require explicit judgment (sarahah), not implied judgment. The disjunctive does not explicitly state a conditional relation, nor does the conditional explicitly state opposition. Therefore the definitions are not interchangeable. He extends this to the categorical proposition: its definition (judgment of something of something) does not apply to conditionals because conditionals only imply such a judgment, not state it explicitly. Hence, the original definitions remain valid.

Key Terms
نقد (naqd): An objection raised against a definition or argument.
جواب (jawab): The answer or rebuttal to an objection.
صراحة (sarahah): Explicitness; the quality of being directly stated rather than implied.
استلزام (istilzam): Implication; the logical consequence that is intended but not explicit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's response, why does 'Either this number is even or odd' not fit the definition of a conditional?
A) Because it is not a conditional in form.
B) Because it does not explicitly state a relation on a condition.
C) Because it is false.
D) Because it only implies a conditional.

Key Takeaway
The definitions of conditional and disjunctive propositions rely on explicit (sarahah) judgment, not implied meaning; thus an implied conditional does not make a disjunctive a conditional.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1) Reconstruct the objector's argument using a fiqh example: 'Either this act is obligatory or forbidden' (disjunctive) also fits the definition of a conditional because it implies 'If it is obligatory, then it is not forbidden' etc. Then explain why the author would reject this, citing the sarahah principle.
?
2) Find a conditional statement in a fiqh text (e.g., 'If water is pure, then it purifies') and identify whether the implied categorical judgment (pure water purifies) is explicit or implicit. Write a brief analysis using the terms sarahah and istilzam.

Rational vs. Inductive Classification in Logic
Author critiques rational basis for dividing propositions, concluding it is inductive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between rational (hasr 'aqli) and inductive (hasr istiqra'i) classification and identify the type of the categorical-conditional division.

Core Idea
Not every logical division is rationally necessary; some are based on induction.

In this passage, the author examines whether the division of propositions into categorical and conditional is rationally necessary or merely based on observation. He concludes that it is inductive, because the intellect cannot rule out the possibility of a third type by pure reason. This lesson will help you understand the criterion for a rational division and why this distinction matters in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of division
The author distinguishes between rational division (hasr 'aqli), where the intellect deems a third part impossible, and inductive division (hasr istiqra'i), which relies on empirical observation.
2
Understand the criterion for rational division
A rational division must be exhaustive between affirmation and negation (al-nafy wa al-ithbat). That is, the negation of one part implies the affirmation of the other, and vice versa.
Example: the division of being into necessary and contingent is rational because denying necessity implies contingency.
3
Apply the criterion to the categorical-conditional division
The definition of a categorical proposition is 'a judgment of something of something' and a conditional is 'otherwise'. But 'otherwise' does not logically contradict the categorical definition; a third type is not ruled out by reason alone.
The text says 'this criterion is not present in the division'.
4
Conclude that the division is inductive
Since we only know through experience (istiqra') that no other types of propositions exist, the division is inductive, not rational.
The author supports this by noting the commentator's cautious phrasing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(19) قوله واعلم ان حصر القضية في الحملية والشرطية على ما قرره المصنف: حيث قال: فان كان الحكم فيها بثبوت شيء لشيء او نفيه عنه فحملية والافشرطية» أى وان لم يكن الحكم في القضية بثبوت شيء لشيء او نفيه عنه وانت ترى ان هذا المعنى الذى هو مفاد قوله - والا لا يستفاد منه معنى القضية الشرطية كما هو واضح فكيف يكون حصر القضية على ما قرره المصنف في الحملية والشرطية حصراً عقلياً دائراً بين النفى والاثبات؟ فان الدائر بين النفى والاثبات يعرف نفيه من مجرد اثباته واثباته من مجرد نفيه فانه اذا قبل سلب یعنی لا ايجاب او ايجاب يعنى لا سلب، وليس هذا الملاك موجوداً في تقسيم القضية الى الحملية والشرطية. نعم لم نعثر في الخارج على قضية سوى الحملية و الشرطية كما لم تعثر على قسم آخر للشرطية غير الاتصال و الانفصال. فهذا كله نتيجة الاستقراء ، لابرهان العقل. وقد يكون لهذه العلة قال الشارح: على ما قرره المصنف» ولم يقطع به كما لم ينسبه الى نفسه او الى جمهور المناطقة. (20) قوله و اعلم ان حصر القضية ... اعلم ان الحصر بحكم العقل قسمان: عقلی مردد بين النفى والاثبات و استقرائي ليس كذلك ، لانه اذا انحصر شي في قسمين مثلاً فلا يخلواما ان يكون يمتنع عند العقل أن يوجد له قسم آخر أو لا يمتنع فالاول يسمى بالحصر العقلي لانه يحصل بحكم العقل و جزمه والثانى بالاستقرائي لانه انما حصل بسبب الاستقراء والتتبع دون حكم العقل بل هو يحكم بحيث
(19) His saying: 'Know that the division of the proposition into categorical and conditional as the author determined: where he said: If the judgment in it is of affirming something of something or negating it from it, then it is categorical, otherwise it is conditional.' That is, if the judgment in the proposition is not of affirming something of something or negating it from it. And you see that this meaning which is the import of his saying – otherwise – does not provide the meaning of the conditional proposition, as is clear. So how can the division of the proposition as the author determined into categorical and conditional be a rational division that is exhaustive between negation and affirmation? For what is exhaustive between negation and affirmation is known such that its negation is known from its mere affirmation and its affirmation from its mere negation, because if it accepts negation, that means it is not affirmation, or affirmation means it is not negation. And this criterion is not present in the division of the proposition into categorical and conditional. Indeed, we have not found in existence a proposition other than categorical and conditional, just as we have not found another division of the conditional other than connected and disjunctive. So all this is the result of induction, not rational proof. And perhaps for this reason the commentator said: 'as the author determined' and did not assert it definitively, just as he did not attribute it to himself or to the majority of logicians. (20) His saying: 'Know that the division of the proposition...' Know that division by rational judgment is of two types: rational [division] that is exhaustive between negation and affirmation, and inductive [division] that is not so. For if something is divided into, say, two parts, either it is impossible for the intellect that there exists another part, or it is not impossible. The first is called rational division because it is obtained by the judgment and certainty of the intellect, and the second is called inductive because it is obtained only by induction and investigation, not by the judgment of the intellect – rather it judges that ...
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage examines the nature of the division of propositions into categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (shartiyya). The author questions whether this division is rational (aqlī) or inductive (istiqrā'ī). He notes that the author (al-Muṣannif) defined a categorical as a judgment of something of something, and a conditional as otherwise. But this definition does not provide a clear contradictory opposite; the division is not like the division into affirmation and negation, where one is known from the other by simple contradiction. Instead, we only know through experience (istiqrā') that there are only these two types of propositions, and within conditionals only connected and disjunctive. Therefore, the division is inductive, not rational. He supports this by noting that the commentator (al-Shāriḥ) said 'as the author determined' without asserting it as definitive. Then he clarifies the two types of division: rational division is where the intellect deems it impossible for a third part to exist (like division of being into necessary and contingent), while inductive division is based on empirical observation and is not necessary. Thus the division of propositions is inductive.

حصر استقرائي
استقراء

Key Takeaway
The division of propositions into categorical and conditional is inductive, not rationally necessary, because we cannot conceive a third type by rational necessity alone.

Rational Division of Propositions
Author explains rational division of propositions into categorical and conditional based on judgment inclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a rational division of propositions from a non-rational one by testing whether the defining criterion necessarily excludes a middle.

Core Idea
A division is rational only if the mind sees no middle ground between the two types.

In Islamic logic, dividing propositions into categorical and conditional is not merely a matter of convenience. The author insists that his definition makes the division a 'rational enumeration' (hasr aqli), meaning the mind cannot conceive a third type. Here we will see why this is so and how alternative definitions fail to achieve this rationality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being divided
The author divides 'proposition' (qadiyya) into two types: categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (shartiyya).
This is the overarching classification under scrutiny.
2
Extract the defining criterion for each type
According to the author, a categorical proposition is one that includes a judgment of affirmation or negation; a conditional does not. This is the basis of the division.
The criterion is the presence or absence of a judgment (hukm).
3
Test the exhaustiveness of the division
The author argues that any proposition either has a judgment or it does not; there is no middle because that would violate the law of non-contradiction. Thus the division is rational.
The mind necessarily excludes a middle, just as it excludes a third between human and non-human.
4
Compare with alternative definitions
Some define categorical as having singular terms and conditional as having compound terms; others define by resolvability into singulars. These allow a proposition with one side singular and the other compound, which is neither purely categorical nor purely conditional, thus a middle exists.
The author shows that rationality depends on the chosen criterion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاذا تمهد هذا فنقول : ان القضية في الحملية و الشرطية على ما حققه المصنف حضر عقلى لا يجوز العقل وجود الواسطة بينهما، لانا اذا قلنا ان القضية ان اشتملت على الحكم بالثبوت او بالنفى فحملية والافشرطية يجزم العقل بعدم وجود الواسطة البتة ضرورة استحالة ارتفاع النقيضين فهو كقولنا: هذا اما ان يكون انساناً ام لا فالعقل يحكم بانحصاره فيهما والايلزم ارتفاع النقيضين واما على ما ذكره بعضهم من ان طرفى القضية ان كانا مفردين حقيقة أو حكماً كما اذا كان الخبر مشتقاً مثلاً فهى الحملية وان كانا مركبين فهى الشرطية فالعقل لا يحكم بالانحصار فيه فانه يجوز ان تكون قضية احد طرقيها مفرد والآخر مركب غير مأول بالمفرد كما في الحملية، فحينئذ لا يصدق عليها الحملية ولا الشرطية وتبقى واسطة بينهما و يبطل انحصارها فيهما عنده وكذا على ما ذكره بعضهم من ان القضية ان انحلت بطرفيها الى مفردين فحملية وان لم تنحل فشرطية فان العقل يجوز وجود قسم آخر و هو ان تنحل باحد طرفيها الى المفرد دون الاخر فتأمل.
When this is established, we say: the proposition regarding categorical and conditional, according to what the author verified, is a rational enumeration; the mind does not permit the existence of a middle between them, because when we say that if the proposition includes a judgment of affirmation or negation, it is categorical, and otherwise it is conditional, the mind is certain of the absence of a middle necessarily, due to the impossibility of the rising of the two contradictories. It is like our saying: 'This is either a human or not'; the mind judges its restriction to them, otherwise the two contradictories would rise. But according to what some mentioned, that if the two sides of the proposition are singular in reality or by ruling, as when the predicate is derived, etc., then it is categorical; and if they are compound, then it is conditional; the mind does not judge the restriction to them, because it is possible that there be a proposition one of whose sides is singular and the other compound, not interpreted as singular as in the categorical. Then it is not applicable to it as categorical nor conditional, and a middle remains between them, and its restriction to them is invalid. Likewise according to what some mentioned, that if the proposition is resolved by its two sides into two singulars, it is categorical; and if it is not resolved, then it is conditional; then the mind permits the existence of another division, which is that it is resolved by one of its sides into a singular but not the other. So reflect.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage defines the division of propositions into categorical and conditional as a rational (aqli) enumeration. The author, al-Muṣannif, holds that the division admits no middle ground because the mind, upon considering whether a proposition includes a judgment of affirmation or negation, necessarily restricts it to one of the two types. This is like the rational division of a thing into either a human or not, which is exhaustive. The author then contrasts his view with two alternative definitions: one based on whether the terms are singular or compound, and another based on whether the proposition resolves into singular terms. On these alternative definitions, the division is not rational because a middle case is conceivable (e.g., one side singular and the other compound). The passage thus justifies the author's definition as the only one that yields a true rational division.

Key Terms
حصر عقلي
حملية
شرطية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is based on the number of terms.

C) Because it is based on whether the predicate is derived.
D) Because it is based on resolvability into singulars.

Key Takeaway
A division is rational if the mind necessarily excludes a middle, which for propositions occurs when the defining criterion is inclusion vs. non-inclusion of affirmation/negation.

Empirical Division of Conditional Propositions
Author states that the division of conditional into conjunctive and disjunctive is empirical, not rational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the division of conditional propositions into conjunctive and disjunctive is empirical, not rational, and identify the logical basis for the classification.

Core Idea
The division of conditional propositions is empirical because exhaustive investigation found no other relation.

In logic, conditional propositions are classified into conjunctive (if-then) and disjunctive (either-or). But is this classification necessary or based on experience? The author argues that it is empirical, because the mind could conceive other relations, yet none have been found in actual sciences and language. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criterion for conditional propositions
Recall that a conditional proposition does not make a direct affirmation or negation; it expresses a relation between two statements. The author affirms this as the defining feature.
2
Analyze the logical possibility of other relations
Consider whether the mind could conceive a conditional judgment that is neither conjunctive nor disjunctive. The author says the mind permits other possibilities, e.g., a judgment of pure temporal sequence.
3
Evaluate the empirical evidence
The author states that after exhaustive investigation (istiqra) of the theoretical sciences and customary language, no such alternative was found. This empirical survey grounds the division.
4
Connect to the author's phrasing
Notice that in his later division, the author uses conditional phrases like 'if... then' rather than an exclusive 'either... or', subtly acknowledging the empirical basis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و اما حصر الشرطية على المتصلة والمنفصلة فاستقرائي لان المعتبر في الشرطية ان لا يحكم فيها بالثبوت والنفى ولا يلزم من ذلك ان لا يحكم الا بالاتصال او الانفصال بل يجوز العقل ان يكون الحكم بوجه آخر غير الاتصال والانفصال لكن لم يوجد بعد التتبع والاستقراء في العلوم الحكمية ومتعارف اللغة قضية شرطية حكم فيها بوجه آخر سوى الاتصال والانفصال و لذا قال المصنف في تقسيم الشرطية فيما سيأتي: الشرطية متصلة ان كان الحكم فيها بالثبوت على تقدير أخرى أو بنفي ذلك الثبوت و منفصلة ان كان الحكم فيها بالتنافي بين التسبتين أو عدمه ولم يقل : اما متصلة و اما منفصلة اشعاراً بذلك.
As for the enumeration of conditional into conjunctive and disjunctive, it is empirical, because the considered [criterion] in the conditional is that there is no judgment of affirmation or negation; and it does not follow from that that there is no judgment except conjunction or disjunction. Rather, the mind permits that the judgment be in another way besides conjunction and disjunction, but after investigation and empirical search in the theoretical sciences and customary language, a conditional proposition has not been found in which a judgment is made in another way besides conjunction and disjunction. Therefore, the author said in the division of the conditional later: 'The conditional is conjunctive if the judgment in it is affirmation on a condition or negation of that affirmation; and disjunctive if the judgment in it is incompatibility between two affirmations or its absence.' He did not say 'either conjunctive or disjunctive' to indicate this.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage distinguishes the division of conditional propositions into conjunctive and disjunctive as empirical (istiqrai), not rational. The criterion for a conditional is the absence of a direct affirmative or negative judgment. The author argues that while the mind could conceive of other types of relation (e.g., a judgment of temporal sequence without implication), exhaustive investigation has not found any such proposition in the sciences or language. Therefore, the enumeration is based on empirical observation. The author's own later definition (quoted) uses conditional phrases like 'if the judgment is... then it is conjunctive; if... then disjunctive' rather than saying 'either conjunctive or disjunctive,' which subtly acknowledges the empirical basis.

Key Terms
استقرائي
متصلة
منفصلة

C) Because it is based on the number of terms.
D) Because it is based on affirmation/negation.

Key Takeaway
The division of conditional propositions is based on empirical investigation, not rational necessity.

Author's Neutral Terms for Conditional Parts
Author explains neutral terminology for conditional parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the author uses 'first part' and 'second part' instead of 'antecedent' and 'consequent' and summarize the grammatical-logical dispute that motivates this choice.

Core Idea
Neutral terms avoid disciplinary bias in conditional analysis.

In this passage, the author of the hashiya defends his decision to replace the standard logical terms 'antecedent' and 'consequent' with 'first part' and 'second part'. He does this to avoid taking sides in a disagreement between grammarians and logicians about which part of a conditional is the subject and which is the predicate. Understanding this choice reveals how logic adapts to language and disciplinary conventions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the standard terms
Recall that logicians usually call the condition 'antecedent' (مقدم) and the consequent 'consequent' (تالي). The author replaces these with 'first part' (جزء أول) and 'second part' (جزء ثان).
2
Recognize the dispute
Grammarians analyze the conditional 'If A then B' as: B is the subject, 'exists' is predicate, and A is a restriction. Logicians treat the whole conditional as a judgment of implication: A is subject, B is predicate. Thus they disagree on subject/predicate assignment.
See the example: 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists.'
3
Understand the author's solution
By using 'first part' and 'second part', the author avoids committing to either analysis. His terms are acceptable to both grammarians and logicians, making his work universally useful.
He says: 'So he deviated from that to be correct for both groups and acceptable on both views.'
4
Apply the concept
Now explain the significance: This neutrality prevents unnecessary disciplinary conflict and allows the logical structure to be taught without prior grammatical commitments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم ان المصنف عدل عما هو المشهور عند الجمهور حيث عبر عن المحكوم عليه و به بالجزء الاول و الثانى و المشهور عندهم هو التعبير بالاولين و ذلك لان بين اهل العربية والميزان خلافاً في تعيين المحكوم عليه و به فى القضية الشرطية على ما ذكره المصنف في شرح التلخيص. و حاصله انه اذا قلنا مثلاً : « ان كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود فعند أهل العربية «النهار» محکوم علیه و «موجود» محکوم به والشرط قيد له و مفهوم القضية ان الوجود يثبت للنهار على تقدير طلوع الشمس و ظاهر ان الجزاء باق على ما كان عليه من احتمال الصدق والكذب وصدقها باعتبار مطابقة الحكم بثبوت الوجود للنهارح وكذبها بعدمها وعند اهل الميزان المحكوم عليه هو الشرط والمحكوم به هو الجزاء و مفهوم القضية الحكم بلزوم الجزاء للشرط وصدقها باعتبار مطابقة الحكم باللزوم و كذبها بعدمها، فلو قال هنا أن المحكوم عليه يسمى مقدماً والمحكوم به تالياً، لما كان صحيحاً عند اهل العربية واختص يما ذهب اليه ارباب الميزان فعدل عن ذلك ليصح عند الفريقين ويكون مقبولاً على المذهبين فكانه قال : ان الجزء الأول اعنى الشرط يسمى مقدماً سواء قلنا بانه المحكوم عليه كما ذهب اليه المنطقيون او قيداً للمسند كما ذهب اليه النحويون، والجزء الثانى اعنى: الجزاء يسمى تالياً سواء قلنا ايضاً بانه المحكوم به على ما هو المعتبر عند الفرقة الاولى او مجموع المحكوم عليه و به كما هو المعتبر عند الثانية.
Then know that the author deviated from what is well-known among the majority, for he expressed the subject and predicate with 'first part' and 'second part,' whereas the well-known among them is the expression with 'the two firsts.' That is because there is disagreement between the grammarians and logicians in specifying the subject and predicate in the conditional proposition, as the author mentioned in his commentary on Talkhīṣ. The summary is that if we say, for example, 'If the sun is shining, then the daytime exists': according to the grammarians, 'daytime' is the subject and 'exists' is the predicate, and the condition is a restriction on it; the concept of the proposition is that existence is affirmed of daytime on the condition of sunrise; clearly the consequent remains as it is with the possibility of truth and falsity; its truth is by correspondence of the judgment affirming existence for daytime, and falsity by its absence. According to the logicians, the subject is the condition and the predicate is the consequent; the concept of the proposition is the judgment of the implication of the consequent for the condition; its truth is by correspondence of the judgment of implication, and falsity by its absence. So if he had said here that the subject is called antecedent and the predicate consequent, it would not be correct according to the grammarians, and it would be specific to the view of the logicians. So he deviated from that to be correct for both groups and acceptable on both views. As if he said: the first part—meaning the condition—is called antecedent, whether we say it is the subject as the logicians hold, or a restriction on the predicate as the grammarians hold; and the second part—meaning the consequent—is called consequent, whether we say it is the predicate as according to the first group, or the combination of subject and predicate as according to the second group.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage explains the author's choice of neutral terminology for the parts of a conditional proposition. Instead of calling the condition 'antecedent' (مقدم) and the consequent 'consequent' (تالي), which are common among logicians, the author uses 'first part' (جزء اول) and 'second part' (جزء ثان). This avoids the disagreement between grammarians (who consider the consequent's components as subject and predicate) and logicians (who take the entire condition as subject and the entire consequent as predicate). By using neutral terms, the author's definitions are acceptable to both groups. The passage cites the author's own explanation from his commentary on Talkhīṣ.

Key Terms

جزء ثان
مقدم
تالي

The author's shift to neutral terminology for conditional parts shows how logic adapts to language and interdisciplinary harmony, avoiding unnecessary conflicts over grammatical roles.

Classifying Propositions by Subject: Personal, Natural, Quantified, Suspended
Gloss defining natural, quantified, and suspended propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a proposition as personal, natural, quantified, or suspended based on its subject and quantifier.

Core Idea
Four types of propositions based on subject: personal (real particular), natural (nature itself), quantified (explicit quantity), suspended (quantity unmentioned).

In Islamic logic, propositions are classified by the type of subject. This lesson covers the four main categories: personal, natural, quantified, and suspended. This classification is crucial for understanding how propositions function in syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
Look at the subject term: is it a real particular (like Zaid) or a nature (like 'human')? If it's a real particular, it may be personal.
A real particular refers to a specific individual, not a class.
2
Check for quantifier
Is there an explicit quantifier like 'every' or 'some'? If yes, it is quantified (maḥṣūra). If no, it is either natural or suspended.
3
Distinguish natural and suspended
If the subject is a nature (e.g., 'human') and no quantifier is mentioned, it is natural (ṭabīʿiyya). If the subject is not a nature but still unquantified, it is suspended (muhmala).
A suspended proposition has an undetermined quantity.
4
Apply to kalam examples
Classify: 'Zaid is standing' (personal), 'Human is an animal' (natural), 'Every human is mortal' (quantified), 'A man came' (suspended).
Practice with theological and legal examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۲۳) :ای فیسمى ما موضوعه طبيعة طبيعية وما بين فيه كمية افراد الموضوع «محصورة» و ما لم يبين «مهملة» وهكذا الأمر في اقسام المحصورات. (۲۴) قوله: « كقولنا هذا انسان» في التمثيل بذلك دون قولنا زيد «قائم» و نحوه اشعار بان الشخصية ما يكون موضوعه جزئياً حقيقياً اعم من ان يكون ذلك بحسب أصل الوضع او في الاستعمال كاسماء الاشارة والضماير على مذهب المصنف، فلايرد ان نحو هذا) (انسان) و هو انسان ليس بمندرج تحت الشخصية على رأى المصنف فان اسماء الاشارة و الضماير و نحوها موضوعة عنده للمعاني الكلية، نعم يكون مندرجاً تحتها عند من يقول بكون الوضع فيها خاصاً ايضاً فتأمل. (ميرزا محمد علی) (۲۵) قوله و على الثانى...» ظا .... ظاهره كغيره ان الموضوع في المحصورة يجب ان يكون كلياً فعليه يلزم ان لا يكون نحو قولنا كل حیوان ناطق انسان مما الموضوع فيه مركب غير جزئى من المحصورات و لاشك انه ليس بشخصية ولا طبيعية ولا مهملة ايضاً فيلزم الواسطة وعبارة المصنف سالمة من ذلك كما
(23) That is, that whose subject is a natural nature is called 'natural,' and that in which the quantity of the individuals of the subject is mentioned is called 'quantified,' and that which is not clarified is called 'suspended.' And so is the case in the divisions of quantified [propositions]. (24) His saying: 'like our saying this is a human' — in giving this example instead of our saying 'Zaid is standing' and the like, there is an indication that the personal is that whose subject is a real particular, whether that is according to original formation or in usage, like demonstratives and pronouns according to the author's view. So it is not objected that something like 'this is a human' is not included under the personal according to the author's view, because in his view demonstratives and pronouns are placed for universal meanings. Yes, it would be included under it according to those who say that the formation in them is also specific. So reflect. (Mīrzā Muḥammad ʿAlī) (25) His saying: 'and on the second...' Its apparent meaning, like others, is that the subject in the quantified must be universal. According to this, it would be necessary that a saying like 'every speaking animal is a human' — where the subject is compound, not singular — is not among the quantifeds, and there is no doubt that it is neither personal, nor natural, nor suspended, so a middle would be required. But the author's phrasing is safe from that...
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This set of glosses discusses the classification of propositions based on their subject. Gloss (23) defines: a proposition whose subject is a natural nature is called 'natural' (ṭabīʿiyya); if the quantity (e.g., 'every,' 'some') is specified, it is 'quantified' (maḥṣūra); if not, it is 'suspended' (muhmala). Gloss (24) clarifies that 'personal' (shakhsiyya) propositions have a real particular subject, and the author's example 'this is a human' uses a demonstrative, which the author considers universal in meaning; thus it is not personal on his view. Gloss (25) raises a problem: if the subject in quantified propositions must be universal (simple term), then a compound subject like 'every speaking animal' would not fit into personal, natural, or suspended, creating a middle. The gloss suggests the author's phrasing avoids this issue. The text is incomplete but provides insight into the precision of classification.

Nuances: Demonstratives and Compound Subjects
Gloss clarifying personal propositions and addressing compound subject problem

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why 'this is a human' is not a personal proposition according to the author, and analyze the potential middle in classifying compound subjects like 'every speaking animal'.

Core Idea
Demonstratives are universals in disguise; compound subjects challenge the neat tripartite division.

Closer examination reveals that the personal proposition has a narrow definition: only real particulars like 'Zaid'. Demonstratives like 'this' are considered universal by the author. Also, compound subjects like 'every speaking animal' may not fit neatly into personal, natural, or quantified, creating a potential gap. This lesson explores these nuances from glosses (24) and (25).

Guided Path
1
Understand the author's view on demonstratives
The author holds that demonstratives and pronouns are placed for universal meanings. Thus, 'this' indicates a universal concept, not a real particular.
This differs from views that consider demonstratives as particulars.
2
Apply to the example
The proposition 'This is a human' has a demonstrative subject, which is universal in the author's view, so it is not a personal proposition.
3
Identify the compound subject issue
A proposition like 'Every speaking animal is human' has a compound subject ('speaking animal'). This subject is not a real particular (so not personal), nor a simple nature (so not natural), nor is its quantity unmentioned (it is quantified), but the subject is not a simple universal term. It would thus not fit the standard three categories.
The gloss says this would create a 'middle' (wasiṭa) — a category that is neither personal, natural, nor suspended.
4
Consider the solution
The gloss notes that the author's phrasing in the matn is safe from this issue, implying that the definition of quantified propositions may allow compound subjects, or that the compound subject can still be considered a universal nature.

Defending a Definition Dialectically
Author defends the definition of categorical proposition against a potential objection from conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's dialectical defense of the definition of categorical proposition and evaluate its strength.

Core Idea
A definition stands unless a stronger alternative is proven necessary.

In this passage, the author responds to an objection that the definition of categorical proposition is invalidated by conditional propositions. The defense uses a principle of charitable interpretation: assume the definition is correct unless proven otherwise, and prefer to combine views when possible. This is a common dialectical strategy in Islamic logic and legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the position being defended
Find the definition or statement the author is protecting. Here, it is the definition of a categorical proposition (al-ḥamliyya).
The definition itself is not given; the author assumes it is already known.
2
Identify the potential objection
The objection is that the definition of a categorical proposition might be invalidated by conditional propositions (al-sharṭiyya).
The objection is implied: why deviate from the standard definition? The author refutes this grounds.
3
Analyze the author's response
The author uses two steps: first, assuming the definition is correct, it is not invalidated by conditionals. Second, even if there is a competing view, combining both is preferable unless one is extremely weak.
This reflects the principle of jamʿ (reconciliation) in Islamic scholarship.
4
Evaluate the strength of the defense
Assess whether the author successfully rebuts the objection. The defense relies on an assumption of correctness and a preference for harmony, which may be acceptable in dialectical contexts but not necessarily conclusive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرض التسليم لا نتقض تعريف الحملية بالشرطية كما هو ظاهر فحينئذ لا يصح ما ذكر وجهاً للعدول عن عبارة القوم . ويمكن ان يقال انه وان كان المعتبر عنده هنا ما ذهب اليه المنطقيون، الا ان الجمع بين المذهبين و ان كان على الظاهر مهما امكن اولى من اختيار احدهما كما هو ظاهر لمن له ادني درية بسياق الكلام الا ان يكون القول الآخر ضعيفاً في غاية السقوط بحيث لا يعتنى لشأنه ولا يلتفت الى ماله اليه اللهم فتأمل. (میرزا محمد علی)
Assuming [the definition] does not invalidate the definition of the categorical by the conditional, as is apparent. So then what was mentioned as a justification for deviating from the statement of the people is not valid. And it can be said that even if the authoritative [opinion] here is what the logicians have gone to, yet combining the two views, if possible in appearance, is more appropriate than choosing one of them, as is apparent to anyone with the slightest awareness of the context of the discourse, unless the other statement is extremely weak and collapses to such an extent that it is not taken into account and no attention is paid to what it points to. O Allah, so reflect. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical defense of the definition of the categorical proposition. The author begins by stating that if we assume the definition (of the categorical) is correct, it is not invalidated by the conditional proposition, as is apparent. Therefore, the objection that would justify deviating from the standard definition is not valid. Then the author offers a general principle: even if the authoritative position here is what the logicians have adopted, it is better to combine both views if possible, unless the other view is extremely weak. The author ends with a caution to reflect.

Identifying Antecedent and Consequent in Conditionals
Author responds to an objection about naming the parts of a conditional proposition when linguistic order differs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the antecedent and consequent of a conditional proposition regardless of their linguistic order.

Core Idea
The antecedent is defined by logical priority, not by position in speech.

A common confusion in logic is that the antecedent must always come first in a sentence. This passage addresses that confusion by examining a conditional like 'I honor you if you come.' We'll see how the author insists that the naming is based on logical function, not word order, and explore three distinct arguments to defend this principle.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
The objector points out a conditional (e.g., 'I honor you if you come') where the first part ('I honor you') is logically the consequent, yet is called 'antecedent' because it appears first. They argue this makes the naming inaccurate.
Imagine if 'if you come' came first; then the first part would be the antecedent. The objection is about consistency.
2
Identify the author's first response
The author denies that the antecedent in that example is the second part ('you come'). Instead, he claims the true antecedent is omitted but implied by the context. This means the linguistic form does not capture the logical structure fully.
This response relies on a deep analysis of meaning beyond surface structure.
3
Identify the second response
Even if we concede the grammatical point (as some grammarians do), the naming is based on the original logical order: condition first, then result. The original order is priority, even if reversal is allowed.
This is like saying that 'antecedent' always refers to the part that is logically prior, regardless of actual order.
4
Identify the third response
Alternatively, the naming can be based on the majority of instances. Since most conditionals place the antecedent first, it is safe to call the first part the antecedent by default.
This is a practical argument: general usage overrides exceptional cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال: ان الجزء الاول قديتأخر نحو أكرمك ان جئتنى فلا يصح تسميته «مقدما» مطلقا وكذلك لا يصح تسمية الثاني «تالياً» مطلقا لانه قد يقدم كما ذكر. لانا لانسلم ان المقدم فى المثال هو الجزء الثانى بل هو محذوف بقرينته. ولو سلم كما ذهب اليه بعض النحاة فنقول : التسمية بملاحظة الأصل ولاريب ان الاصل هو تقديم الجزء الاول وتأخير الجزء الثاني وان قلنا بجواز العكس ايضاً أو نقول : هي بملاحظة اغلب الافراد فافهم. (میرزا محمد علی)
It cannot be said: The first part may be delayed, as in 'I honor you if you come', so it is not correct to call it 'antecedent' absolutely, and similarly it is not correct to call the second 'consequent' absolutely because it may be brought forward as mentioned. For we do not concede that the antecedent in the example is the second part; rather, it is omitted by implication. And if it is conceded, as held by some grammarians, then we say: The naming is with regard to the original [order], and there is no doubt that the original is the advancement of the first part and the delay of the second, even if we allow the opposite also. Or we say: It is with regard to the majority of instances. So understand. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage addresses a terminological issue in conditional propositions. An objector says that in a conditional like 'I honor you if you come', the first part (I honor you) is the apodosis, not the protasis, so calling it 'antecedent' is inaccurate. The author responds by denying that the antecedent in this example is the second part; instead, the true antecedent is omitted but implied by context. Alternatively, if one concedes the point as some grammarians do, the naming is based on the original logical order (condition then result) even if linguistic reversal occurs, or based on the majority of cases. The author advises careful reflection.

Key Terms
المقدم
التالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the antecedent (muqaddam) in the sentence 'I honor you if you come'?
A) 'I honor you'
B) 'you come'
C) Omitted by implication
D) Both 'I honor you' and 'you come'

Key Takeaway
The antecedent (muqaddam) is the part that expresses the condition, regardless of whether it appears first or last in the sentence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement: 'If this world had two gods, they would not be able to create order.' Identify the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tālī) according to logical priority, not word order.
?
Find a similar conditional in a fiqh text (e.g., 'If one is traveling, one may shorten the prayer.') and explain why the antecedent is the condition for the ruling.

Propositions

Definition of Natural, Quantified, and Unquantified Propositions
Author defines three types of proposition based on the nature of the subject and whether quantity is specified.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between natural, quantified, and unquantified propositions based on the subject and quantity specification.

Core Idea
The quantity of the subject determines if a proposition is quantified or not; natural propositions ignore individuals entirely.

In this passage, the author clarifies a fundamental classification of propositions based on the subject. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing logical arguments, especially when dealing with definitions or legal rulings that may be unquantified.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basis of classification
Notice that the classification depends on whether the subject refers to a nature (essence) or to individuals, and whether the quantity of individuals is specified.
2
Define the natural proposition
A natural proposition has a subject that is a natural nature—the essence itself without considering individuals. Example: 'Man is an animal' meaning the nature of man.
3
Define the quantified proposition
A quantified proposition specifies the quantity of individuals of the subject, i.e., 'all' or 'some'. Example: 'Every man is mortal' is quantified universal.
4
Define the unquantified proposition
An unquantified proposition does not specify quantity. Example: 'Man is in loss' (from Quran) is unquantified—it does not say 'all men' or 'some men'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ای فیسمى ما موضوعه طبيعة طبيعية وما بين فيه كمية افراد الموضوع «محصورة» و ما لم يبين «مهملة» وهكذا الأمر في اقسام المحصورات.
That is, what has a subject that is a natural nature is called 'natural' (ṭabīʿiyya); what has the quantity of the subject's individuals specified is called 'quantified' (maḥṣūra); and what is not specified is called 'unquantified' (muhmala). And likewise in the divisions of quantified [propositions].
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage defines three types of proposition based on the subject. If the subject is the nature itself without regard to individuals, it is 'natural'. If the quantity of individuals (all or some) is specified, it is 'quantified'. If quantity is not specified, it is 'unquantified'. The author also notes that the same classification applies to the subdivisions of quantified propositions (i.e., universal and particular).

Key Terms
طبيعة طبيعية
محصورة
مهملة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Tabi'iyya (natural)
B) Mahsura (quantified)
C) Muhmala (unquantified)
D) Shakhsiyya (singular)

Key Takeaway
Propositions are classified by whether their subject is a nature (natural), or the quantity of individuals is specified (quantified) or not (unquantified).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Justify each classification based on the subject and quantity.
?
