Propositions
intermediate
10 min
Singular Proposition: Demonstratives as Universals?
Definition of singular proposition with refinement on demonstratives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given proposition qualifies as singular (shakhṣiyya) based on whether its subject is a real particular, considering the matn author's view on demonstratives.

Core Idea
A singular proposition's subject must be a real particular, but demonstratives may be universals by coinage.

In this lesson, we refine the definition of the singular proposition (al-shakhṣiyya). The core criterion is that the subject must be a real particular (juzʾī ḥaqīqī) — an individual not predicable of many. However, there is a nuance regarding demonstratives and pronouns: according to the author of the matn, these are coined for universal meanings, so propositions like 'this is a man' would not be singular. This refinement protects the definition from counterexamples and clarifies the basis of logical classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining characteristic of a singular proposition
The passage states that a singular proposition has a subject that is a real particular (juzʾī ḥaqīqī). This means the subject refers to a specific individual, not a class. Note that this can be due to original coinage (e.g., proper names) or due to usage (e.g., demonstratives in some contexts).
The term 'real particular' excludes universals like 'human' or 'animal'.
2
Understand the two ways a subject can be a real particular
First, by original coinage (bi-ḥasab aṣl al-waḍʿ): the word itself is assigned to a specific individual, like 'Zayd'. Second, by usage (bi-ḥasab al-istiʿmāl): the word is coined as a universal but used to refer to a particular instance, like 'this' pointing to a specific person.
This distinction is crucial for evaluating demonstratives.
3
Analyze the potential counterexample with demonstratives
One might object that 'this is a human' should be singular because 'this' points to a specific individual. However, according to the matn author, demonstratives are coined for universal meanings (maʿānī kulliyya) — they signify a concept that can apply to many, not a specific individual. Thus, such propositions are not singular under his view.
The objection arises if one assumes demonstratives are always particulars.
4
Compare the two positions on demonstratives
The matn author holds that demonstratives and pronouns are coined for universal meanings, so 'this' is a universal term. Others maintain that they are coined specifically for each instance (waḍʿ khāṣṣ), making them real particulars. The passage notes that if one adopts the latter view, then propositions like 'this is a human' would indeed be singular.
This shows how a logical classification can depend on a linguistic theory.
5
Reflect on the implication for proposition classification
The shift in perspective changes which propositions count as singular. This refinement ensures that the definition of shakhṣiyya remains precise and consistent with the author's broader theory of meaning.
Always consider the underlying theory of coinage when classifying propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في التمثيل بذلك دون قولنا زيد «قائم» و نحوه اشعار بان الشخصية ما يكون موضوعه جزئياً حقيقياً اعم من ان يكون ذلك بحسب أصل الوضع او في الاستعمال كاسماء الاشارة والضماير على مذهب المصنف، فلايرد ان نحو هذا) (انسان) و هو انسان ليس بمندرج تحت الشخصية على رأى المصنف فان اسماء الاشارة و الضماير و نحوها موضوعة عنده للمعاني الكلية، نعم يكون مندرجاً تحتها عند من يقول بكون الوضع فيها خاصاً ايضاً فتأمل. (ميرزا محمد علی)
In giving that example rather than 'Zayd is standing' and the like, there is an indication that the singular [proposition] is that whose subject is a real particular, whether that is due to the original coinage or in usage, like demonstratives and pronouns according to the author's school. So it is not objected that something like 'this is a human' and 'he is a human' is not included under the singular in the author's view, because demonstratives and pronouns and the like are, in his view, coined for universal meanings. Yes, it would be included under it according to those who say that the coinage in them is also specific. So reflect. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage discusses the definition of the singular proposition (shakhṣiyya). The author notes that the example used (instead of 'Zayd is standing') suggests that a singular proposition has a real particular subject, whether by original coinage (like proper names) or by usage (like demonstratives). The potential counterexample 'this is human' is addressed: according to the author (the matn author), demonstratives are coined for universal meanings (i.e., 'this' refers to a concept that can apply to many), so such a proposition would not be singular. However, for those who hold that demonstratives have specific coinage (i.e., refer to a particular instance), it would be singular. The author advises reflection.

Key Terms
شخصية
جزئي حقيقي
أسماء الإشارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the matn author's view, is the proposition 'This is a man' considered a singular proposition?
A) Yes, because 'this' points to a specific individual.
B) No, because demonstratives are coined for universal meanings.
C) Yes, because 'man' is a universal term.
D) No, because the predicate is not a particular.

Key Takeaway
Whether a proposition is singular depends on whether its subject is a real particular, and demonstratives may not qualify as such depending on one's theory of linguistic coinage.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition: 'This prayer is obligatory.' According to the matn author's view, is this proposition singular? Explain your reasoning by referencing the coinage of 'this'.
?
